<article id="post-626" class="group post-container entry-content">
	
				
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Complete List of Department Courses</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p>For complete list of undergraduate courses offered by Department of Slavic Languages and Literatures, see the <a href="http://guide.berkeley.edu/undergraduate/departments/slavic-languages-literatures/#coursestext">Berkeley Academic Guide</a>.</p>
<p>For graduate courses (and language courses relevant to the Graduate Program), see the <a class="moz-txt-link-freetext" href="http://guide.berkeley.edu/graduate/schools-departments-graduate-groups/slavic-languages-literatures/#coursestext">Berkeley Academic Guide.</a></p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>Please note that, as of Fall 2015, all of our language courses have been renamed. For example, Elementary Russian was changed from Slavic 1 to Russian 1<em>,</em> Beginning Armenian changed from Eurasian Studies 1A/B to Armenian 1A/B.  On the Online Schedule of Courses (<a href="http://schedule.berkeley.edu/">schedule.berkeley.edu</a>) and the Berkeley Academic Guide (<a href="http://guide.berkeley.edu/">guide.berkeley.edu</a>) search through the specific language, e.g. <strong>Armenian, Bosnian/Croatian/Serbian, Czech, Hungarian, Polish, Russian</strong>, etc.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-626 -->

<article id="post-1987" class="group post-container entry-content">
	
				
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Courses for Non-UC Berkeley Students</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
<p><strong>During the Summer</strong></p>
<p>Our summer courses are generally open for registration by anyone — not just UC Berkeley students.  We offer a variety of language and culture courses each summer, taught by qualified UCB instructors.  All summer course registration goes through the Berkeley Summer Sessions office, whose <a href="http://summer.berkeley.edu">website</a> outlines the enrollment process, tuition fees, and more.</p>
<p><strong>During the Academic Year</strong></p>
<p>While we are unable to offer traditional “auditing” or “sitting in on” a Fall or Spring course, the UC Extension makes it possible for non-UC Berkeley students to enroll in UC Berkeley classes through their <a href="http://extension.berkeley.edu/static/studentservices/concurrent/">Concurrent Enrollment</a> program, pending instructor approval.</p>
<p>Courses through Concurrent Enrollment can be taken for credit/letter grade, pass/no pass, or no credit; registration costs vary depending on the grading option.  For details on Concurrent Enrollment requirements, deadlines, applications and fees, please visit <a href="http://extension.berkeley.edu/static/studentservices/concurrent/">their web site</a> or call 510-642-4111.</p>
<p>For registration purposes, you will need the class number from UC Berkeley’s official <a href="http://schedule.berkeley.edu/%20">online schedule of classes</a>.</p>
<p>A few weeks before the class begins, it is important that the potential Concurrent Enrollment student e-mails the instructor of the course to introduce him or herself and informs the instructor of his or her plans.  Course descriptions with instructor names are posted in the Courses section of this web site.  In order to contact our instructors, please see the <a href="http://slavic.berkeley.edu/people-category/faculty/">People</a> page of this website.</p>
<p>If you want to take a language course, especially if you are unsure which level is right for you, please see <a href="http://slavic.berkeley.edu/courses-2/language-placement/">the Language Placement page</a>.</p>
<p>In general, applications for Concurrent Enrollment are not officially approved and signed by the instructor until the first week of class, as part of the approval is based on the number of spaces available.  Once approved by the instructor, the application will go through an approval process between the Berkeley campus and the Concurrent Enrollment office, and finally the student will be officially enrolled and billed by the UC Extension.</p>
</div>
<p><span data-offset-key="8emho-0-0"> </span></p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-1987 -->

<article id="post-2005" class="group post-container entry-content">
	
				
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Armenian 101A: Continuing Armenian</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p>Myrna Douzjian | TuTh 12:30-2 | 263 Dwinelle | 3 units</p>
<p><strong>L&amp;S Breadth:</strong>  International Studies</p>
<p>T<strong>HE 101A-101B COURSE SEQUENCE BEGINS IN THE FALL TERM ONLY.  </strong></p>
<p>This course further develops students’ Armenian reading, writing, listening and speaking proficiency. Students will hone their language skills through discussions, oral presentations, written assignments, and a variety of readings and viewings (literature, non-fiction, newspaper articles, songs, and short films). Students will also be given the opportunity to choose readings and assignments based on their individual needs and interests. Three hours of class per week. Course may be repeated for credit.</p>
<p><strong>Texts: </strong>TBA</p>
<p><strong>Prerequisites:</strong>  Armenian 1A-1B or consent of instructor.</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-2005 -->

										</div>

<article id="post-2003" class="group post-container entry-content">
	
				
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Armenian 1A: Introductory Armenian</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p>Myrna Douzjian | TuTh 11-12:30 | 89 Dwinelle | 4 units</p>
<p><strong>THE 1A-1B COURSE SEQUENCE BEGINS IN THE FALL TERM ONLY.  </strong></p>
<p>This course is for students who have little or no prior knowledge of Armenian. It introduces students to both Eastern and Western Armenian through comparative lessons. Emphasis is placed on the basics of modern Armenian grammar, as well as the four language skills of listening, speaking, reading and writing.</p>
<p>The course includes audiovisual material that explores Armenian culture, past and present. Teaching is adjusted in accordance with students&#8217; individual needs, goals, and interests.</p>
<p><strong>Texts: </strong>TBA</p>
<p><strong>Prerequisite:</strong>  None.</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-2003 -->

										</div>

<article id="post-2009" class="group post-container entry-content">
	
				
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Bosnian/Croatian/Serbian 117A: Continuing Bosnian/Croatian/Serbian</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p>Antje Postema | MWF 3-4 | B-3 Dwinelle | 4 units</p>
<p><strong>L&amp;S Breadth: </strong> International Studies</p>
<p>Spoken and written language; advanced grammar review. Some discussion of the contemporary sociolinguistic situation as it relates to language use. Short oral reports and writing practice. Grades based on class participation, midterm and final exam.</p>
<p><strong>Tentative Texts:</strong><br />
Ronelle Alexander and Ellen Elias-Bursac, <em>Bosnian, Croatian, Serbian: A Textbook with Exercises and Basic Grammar </em>(required)<br />
Ronelle Alexander, <em>Bosnian, Croatian, Serbian: A Grammar with Sociolinguistic Commentary </em>(recommended)<br />
Other materials will be distributed by the instructor.</p>
<p><strong> Prerequisites: </strong><strong>Bosnian/Croatian/Serbian</strong> 27B or consent of instructor.</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-2009 -->

<article id="post-2007" class="group post-container entry-content">
	
				
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Bosnian/Croatian/Serbian 27A: Introductory Bosnian/Croatian/Serbian</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p>Dominick Lawton | MWF 9-10 | B-37 Dwinelle | 4 units</p>
<p><strong>THE BCS 27A-27B COURSE SEQUENCE BEGINS IN THE FALL TERM ONLY</strong></p>
<p><strong> </strong>An introduction to Bosnian, Croatian, and Serbian with explanation of major differences (from both a linguistic and sociocultural viewpoint) and the common grammatical core. Students develop basic competence in spoken and written language within a cultural and social context. Regular homework assignments, including on-line assignments; in-class unit exams, midterm and final.</p>
<p><strong>Tentative texts: </strong></p>
<p>Ronelle Alexander and Ellen Elias-Bursac, <em>Bosnian, Croatian Serbian: A Textbook with Exercises and Basic Grammar </em>(required)</p>
<p>Ronelle Alexander, <em>Bosnian, Croatian, Serbian: A Grammar with Sociolinguistic Commentary</em> (recommended)</p>
<p>Information about ordering both these books is available at</p>
<p><a href="http://www.bcsgrammarandtextbook.org">http://www.bcsgrammarandtextbook.org</a></p>
<p><strong>Prerequisites:</strong> None for 27A; 27B presumes a knowledge of 27A or equivalent.</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-2007 -->

<article id="post-2014" class="group post-container entry-content">
	
				
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Czech 116A: Introductory Czech</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p>Ellen Langer | MWF 8-9 | 6115 Dwinelle  | 4 units</p>
<p>Grammar review, advanced grammar topics, vocabulary development, improvement of reading, writing, listening, and speaking competence/confidence. Readings consisting of literary texts and short readings in history, music, art, as well as regular readings from the Czech on-line press; grammar notes and exercises to be handed out in class as needed. Students will read in a variety of subject areas to develop a well-rounded vocabulary and historical and cultural framework. Weekly writing assignments such as journal-keeping and short essays or dialogs.  Listening comprehension exercises based on classics of Czech film and on audio CDs/tapes. Written homework, reading assignments, midterm, oral report, and final exam.</p>
<p><strong>Texts:</strong></p>
<p><strong> </strong>Chcete ještě lépe mluvit česky. Remediosová and Čechová. Harry Putz. ISBN: 978-80-86727-16-5</p>
<p>Optional: Josef Fronek, English-Czech, Czech-English Dictionary. Leda: ISBN-13: 978-8073352837</p>
<p><strong>Prerequisites:</strong> Czech 26B or consent of instructor</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-2014 -->

										</div>

<article id="post-2011" class="group post-container entry-content">
	
				
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Czech 26A: Introductory Czech</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p>Ellen Langer | MWF 9-10 | 6115 Dwinelle  | 4 units</p>
<p><strong>(This course is Fall only. Czech 26B, the continuation of a one-year introductory sequence, is offered in Spring.)</strong></p>
<p>Class meets for three hours of Czech instruction per week. Attendance is required, unless excused. Students will acquire basic communicative competence in all four foreign language skills (speaking, listening, reading, and writing), with an emphasis on culture.</p>
<p><strong>Workload:</strong> Approximately five hours of homework per week, including one hour of mandatory computer work), written homework, quizzes, periodic tests, written midterm, and final. By the end of the two-semester sequence, students will have been introduced to all major grammatical features of modern Czech.</p>
<p><strong>Text:</strong> Materials provided by instructor, based on an updatable text.</p>
<p>OPTIONAL reference grammar: Heim, Contemporary Czech. Slavica: ISBN-13: 978-0893570989</p>
<p><strong>Prerequisites:</strong> None.</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-2011 -->

										</div>
			</div><!-- #content -->



	</div><!-- #main -->

<article id="post-2016" class="group post-container entry-content">
	
				
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Hungarian 1A: Introductory Hungarian</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p>Eva Szoke | TuTh 12:30-2 | 6115 Dwinelle | 3 or 4 units</p>
<p><strong>THE HUNGARIAN 1A-1B COURSE SEQUENCE BEGINS IN THE FALL TERM ONLY.  </strong></p>
<p>Hungarian 1A is offered for either three or four units; the additional unit involves extra written and reading assignments.</p>
<p>The course focuses on building basic language skills and practicing interpersonal, interpretive, and communication modes.  It will explore aspects of the Hungarian culture, including contemporary and historical events, literature, pop-culture, and folklore. Teaching reflects the students’ individual needs, goals, and interests.</p>
<p>Class participation, homework, quizzes, and a final project will provide the basis for grading.</p>
<p>Teaching materials include a textbook, handouts, voice recordings, online vocabulary learning tools, and also a variety of original Hungarian written, visual, and audio sources.</p>
<p><strong>Text:</strong></p>
<p>Textbook: <a href="http://www.amazon.com/s/ref%3Dntt_athr_dp_sr_1?_encoding=UTF8&amp;amp;field-author=Jozsef%20Erdos&amp;amp;search-alias=books&amp;amp;sort=relevancerank">József Erdős</a> and <a href="http://www.amazon.com/s/ref%3Dntt_athr_dp_sr_2?_encoding=UTF8&amp;amp;field-author=Csilla%20Prileszky&amp;amp;search-alias=books&amp;amp;sort=relevancerank">Csilla Prileszky</a>: Halló, Itt Magyarország! Student Book (Hungarian Edition) [Paperback] 20014. Akadémia Kiadó. Budapest, Hungary.</p>
<p>ISBN-10: 963059577X, ISBN-13: 978-9630595773</p>
<p><strong>Prerequisites</strong>:  None.</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-2016 -->

<article id="post-2025" class="group post-container entry-content">
	
				
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Polish 115A: Continuing Polish</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p>Katarzyna Zacha | MWF 10-11 | 6307 Dwinelle | 4 units</p>
<p><strong>L&amp;S Breadth:</strong> International Studies</p>
<p><em> </em>The course gives you an opportunity to broaden your knowledge about Polish grammar and how to use it in active communication. The goal of the course is to improve the fluency of your oral and written communication skills in Polish. You will learn through classroom exercises based on a modern textbook, completion of individual and group assignments, work with various audio materials, and some supplementary readings as assigned. The course will contain the following major components: grammar, pronunciation, reading, writing short essays and other short texts, some translation of short texts, and conversation about a variety of topics.</p>
<p>The advanced 115B course, preceded by the fall 115A course, focuses on the modern standard Polish language and is taught in Polish with explanations in English if necessary. The course is designed for students who have completed the 115A course and students with intermediate and advanced skills in understanding and using Polish language. Class attendance as well as active participation in exercises and conversations is expected.</p>
<p>All assignments shall be completed on time. All missed assignments and exams have to be made up for. Your final grade will be based on your accumulation of points through class attendance, completion of homework assignments, midterm exams and final exam. In addition your final grade will be impacted by your active participation and ability to cooperate with fellow students.</p>
<p><strong>Text:</strong></p>
<p><strong> </strong>Polski, Krok Po Kroku: Level 2 (A2/B1): Coursebook for Learning Polish as a Foreign Language (Polish Edition), Authors: Iwona Stempek, Anna Stelmach, ISBN-10: 8393073111</p>
<p><strong>Prerequisites:</strong> Polish 25B or consent of instructor.</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-2025 -->

<article id="post-2023" class="group post-container entry-content">
	
				
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Polish 25A: Introductory Polish</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p>Katarzyna Zacha | MWF 9-10 | 6307 Dwinelle | 4 units</p>
<p><strong>THE 25A-25B COURSE SEQUENCE BEGINS IN THE FALL TERM ONLY.</strong></p>
<p><strong> </strong>The introductory fall 25A course, followed by the spring 25B course, introduces the modern standard Polish language, and is taught in Polish with explanations in English as needed. 25A is meant for beginners with little or no previous knowledge of the Polish language. The course is designed to provide the basic knowledge necessary for active verbal and written communication in Polish and will contain the following major components: grammar, vocabulary, pronunciation, speaking, reading, writing, and short translation exercises.</p>
<p>The final grade will be based on attendance, participation, completion of homework assignments, weekly quizzes, a midterm exam and a comprehensive final.</p>
<p><strong>Text:</strong><strong><br />
</strong>Polski, Krok Po Kroku: Level 1 (A1/A2): Coursebook for Learning Polish as a Foreign Language (Polish Edition) Authors: Iwona Stempek, Anna Stelmach, Aneta Szymkiewicz, Sylwia Dawidek ISBN-10: 8393073103</p>
<p><strong>Prerequisites:</strong> none.</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-2023 -->

<article id="post-2029" class="group post-container entry-content">
	
				
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Russian 1: Elementary Russian</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p>Section 1: Maria Whittle | M-F 11-12 | 187 Dwinelle | 5 units<br />
Section 2: Katie DeWaele | M-F 1-2 | 179 Dwinelle | 5 units</p>
<p><strong>ASSUMES NO PREVIOUS KNOWLEDGE OF RUSSIAN: if you speak Russian at home or have taken Russian before, you must contact Anna Muza: </strong><a href="mailto:amuza@berkeley.edu"><u>amuza@berkeley.edu</u></a><strong> to determine the best placement for you.</strong></p>
<p>Comprehensive program for the study of Russian. Development of communicative skills and cultural and grammatical competence through reading, writing, listening/viewing, and speaking.</p>
<p>Grades based on daily attendance and participation, homework, compositions, chapter and oral tests, and a final (a computerized standardized test that may be taken anytime during the last two weeks up through the regularly scheduled final time and counts for 5% of the grade).</p>
<p><strong>REQUIRED TEXTS: (AVAILABLE THROUGH ASUC’S CAL TEXTBOOKS)</strong></p>
<p>Mezhdu nami (Между нами at: <a href="http://mezhdunami.org/">http://mezhdunami.org</a>), a new free, web-based textbook by Lynne deBenedette, William Comer, Alla Smyslova, and Jonathan Perkins. Students will need to buy the inexpensive Mezhdu nami 1-5 Classroom Activities and Homework Booklets.</p>
<p><strong>Prerequisites:</strong> Russian 1 has no prerequisites as it assumes no previous knowledge of Russian</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-2029 -->

<article id="post-2033" class="group post-container entry-content">
	
				
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Russian 103A: Advanced Russian</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p>Language Coordinator: Anna Muza<br />
Klara Libman | MWF 11-12 | 6115 Dwinelle Hall | 4 units</p>
<p>The course on advanced Russian reading and grammar is meant for students who have completed the lower-division Russian sequence (Russian 1-4). Can be taken by heritage speakers with an adequate language background (a preliminary placement test / interview required). The course will cover less familiar and more challenging areas of Russian grammar and syntax; develop reading fluency and sophisticated vocabulary.</p>
<p>For speaking practice, consider in Spring 2018 Russian 120B: Advanced Russian Conversation and Communication.</p>
<p><strong> Prerequisites:  </strong>Russian 4 or consent of instructor</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-2033 -->

<article id="post-2032" class="group post-container entry-content">
	
				
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Russian 2: Elementary Russian</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p>David Parker | M-F 11-12 | 246 Dwinelle | 5 units</p>
<p><strong>IF YOU HAVE NOT TAKEN THE PREVIOUS SEMESTER HERE (Russian 1), you must contact Anna Muza:  </strong><a href="mailto:amuza@berkeley.edu"><strong><u>amuza@berkeley.edu</u></strong> </a><strong>to determine the best placement for you (which may be this course).</strong></p>
<p>Comprehensive program for the study of Russian. Development of communicative skills and cultural and grammatical competence through reading, writing, listening/viewing, and speaking.</p>
<p>Grades based on daily attendance and participation, homework, compositions, chapter and oral tests, and a final (a computerized standardized test that may be taken anytime during the last two weeks up through the regularly scheduled final time and counts for 5% of the grade).</p>
<p><strong>REQUIRED TEXTS: (AVAILABLE THROUGH ASUC’S CAL TEXTBOOKS</strong></p>
<p>Mezhdu nami (Между нами at: <a href="http://mezhdunami.org/">http://mezhdunami.org</a>), a new free, web-based textbook by Lynne deBenedette, William Comer, Alla Smyslova, and Jonathan Perkins. Students will need to buy the inexpensive Mezhdu nami 6-9 Classroom Activities and Homework Booklets.</p>
<p><strong>Prerequisites:</strong> Russian 1 (formerly Slavic 1) is the prerequisite for Russian 2.</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-2032 -->

<article id="post-475" class="group post-container entry-content">
	
				
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Language Placement</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p>Students who have learned Russian or other Slavic languages elsewhere and those returning from study abroad will need to take a placement test at the beginning of the semester.</p>
<p><strong>~</strong> <strong>Students starting with <em>1st or 2nd year Russian</em></strong> should consult with Lecturer Anna Muza, Russian Language Coordinator (during her office hours or email <a href="mailto:amuza@berkeley.edu">amuza@berkeley.edu</a> for appointment) about placement in the lower-division language courses (Russian 1, 2, 3, 4). Please contact Lecturer Anna Muza for screening and placement in advance of the first week of classes. <em>All consultations/interviews concerning placement in Russian language classes will be held the first week of term: Wed 8/23 2-5 pm and Fri 8/25 2-5 pm.</em><span style="color: #990000;"><strong><br />
</strong></span></p>
<p><strong>~</strong> <strong>Students seeking placement in advanced Russian (third-year Russian, Russian 103A-B, and other advanced courses) or testing for the Letters and Science foreign language requirement for Russian</strong> should consult with Lecturer Anna Muza (during her office hours or email <a href="mailto:amuza@berkeley.edu">amuza@berkeley.edu</a> for appointment). <em>All consultations/interviews concerning placement in Russian language classes will be held the first week of term: Wed 8/23 2-5 pm and Fri 8/25 2-5 pm.</em></p>
<p><strong>~</strong> <strong>Heritage speakers of Russian who want to take Russian language courses</strong> should consult with Lecturer Anna Muza (during her office hours or email <a href="mailto:amuza@berkeley.edu">amuza@berkeley.edu</a> for appointment). <em>All consultations/interviews concerning placement in Russian language classes will be held the first week of term: Wed 8/23 2-5 pm and Fri 8/25 2-5 pm.</em></p>
<p>~ <strong>Heritage speakers of Russian considering a major or minor track requiring Russian</strong> must make an appointment with the Undergraduate Adviser (Major Adviser) to obtain information specific to their individual language requirements and proficiency/placement examination.</p>
<p>~ <strong>Students of East European or Eurasian languages (Bosnian/Croatian/Serbian, Czech, Hungarian, Polish, Armenian)</strong> should consult with the appropriate language instructor for proficiency testing and placement.</p>
<p>~ <strong>Transfer students and those enrolling in study abroad programs</strong> should consult with the Undergraduate Adviser (Major Adviser) regarding course equivalents accepted by the Department.</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-475 -->

										</div>
			</div><!-- #content -->

<article id="post-2097" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Slavic 202: Advanced Academic Russian</h1>
		<p class="section-spacing">
																M 1-3, 6115 Dwinelle. <b>Instructor: Anna Muza</b>.
								
								</p>
							
									
							
							
														
						
															<p class="ls-breadth"><span class="units">Units: 3</span>
										</p>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">

	
		<p>Russian language course for graduate students in the field of Russian studies. Focus on idiomatic vocabulary, stylistic awareness, academic discourse, and advanced conversation. The class is based on close reading and discussion of original texts of different genres.</p>
<p><strong> </strong>All materials provided by the instructor.</p>
<p>Prerequisites: Graduate standing; consent of instructor.</p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-2097 -->

			
		
		</div><!-- #content-container -->



	</div><!-- #main -->

<article id="post-2142" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Slavic 100L: Advanced Readings in Russian, East European, and Eurasian Languages</h1>
		<p class="section-spacing">
																Day/Time TBA, Instructor's Office. <b>Instructor: Various</b>.
								
								</p>
							
									
							
							
														
						
															<p class="ls-breadth"><span class="units">Units: 1</span>
										</p>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">

	
		<p>Additional readings in the original language to be coordinated with an appropriate upper division lecture course with readings in English offered by the Department of Slavic Languages and Literatures. (This includes all courses in the Slavic 130, 140, 150, 160, and 170 series and Armenian 120 series.) Supervised by the instructor of the lecture course in which the student is also enrolled. Students attend lectures and do all assigned written work in the main lecture course and also perform additional work by reading all or some of the primary texts in the original language. A take-home final examination testing reading comprehension is required. Aimed at Department majors and minors, with consent of instructor, the course is also open to other qualified students interested in Russian, East European, and Eurasian Studies.</p>
<p><strong>Prerequisites: </strong>Consent of instructor. Knowledge of an appropriate Slavic, East European, or Eurasian language with approval of the instructor. Concurrent enrollment in an upper-division class in the Department of Slavic Languages and Literatures.</p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-2142 -->

			
		
		</div><!-- #content-container -->

<article id="post-2098" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Slavic 242: Eighteenth-Century Russian Literature</h1>
		<p class="section-spacing">
																W 3-6, 6115 Dwinelle. <b>Instructor: Luba Golburt</b>.
								
								</p>
							
									
							
							
														
						
															<p class="ls-breadth"><span class="units">Units: 4</span>
										</p>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">

	
		<p>The course introduces students to key literary figures and genres of the Russian eighteenth century. Following a roughly chronological order and focusing on the writings of Feofan Prokopovich, Antiokh Kantemir, Trediakovskii, Lomonosov, Sumarokov, Fonvizin, Catherine the Great, Derzhavin, Radishchev, and Karamzin, we will also consider key issues in Russian early modern culture: Europeanization, its innovations and discontents; the meanings and consequences of modernity; the rise of literature and its complex interactions with the state; the problems associated with foreign influence, cultural reception, transmission, and transformation, as well as ideas of cultural rupture and continuity. While the Russian eighteenth century is often perceived by contemporary readers and students as outdated or prohibitively distant, our task will also be to think about its persistent legacies, and some of our readings will come from later periods and reflect on the eighteenth century’s <em>Nachleben</em>.</p>
<p><strong>Prerequisites:</strong> Graduate standing.</p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-2098 -->

			
		
		</div><!-- #content-container -->



	</div><!-- #main -->

	<footer id="footer" class="wideness" role="contentinfo">

<article id="post-2096" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Slavic 200: Graduate Colloquium</h1>
		<p class="section-spacing">
																M 4-7, Location TBA. <b>Instructor: Eric Naiman</b>.
								
								</p>
							
									
							
							
														
						
															<p class="ls-breadth"><span class="units">Units: 0</span>
										</p>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">

	
		<p>Reports on current scholarly work by faculty and graduate students.  Must be taken on a satisfactory/unsatisfactory basis.  Graduate students must enroll in this course every semester in residence.</p>
<p><strong>Prerequisites:</strong>  Restricted to graduate students in the Slavic department.</p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-2096 -->

<article id="post-2099" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Slavic 280, Section 1: Graduate Seminar: Evgenii Onegin</h1>
		<p class="section-spacing">
																Th 3-6, 6115 Dwinelle. <b>Instructor: Harsha Ram</b>.
								
								</p>
							
									
							
							
														
						
															<p class="ls-breadth"><span class="units">Units: 4</span>
										</p>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">

	
		<p>This seminar seeks to acquaint students with various approaches to literary scholarship. Numerous methodologies, both Russian and Western, will be introduced and tested with respect to the questions they inherently pose and with respect to their applicability to Pushkin’s <em>Evgenii Onegin</em>, the primary text we will be working with over the course of the semester. We shall be asking such questions as: what is a text, philologically and theoretically speaking? What, if any, are its boundaries? What is the relationship between text, intertext and context? What is an author? How does the classic notion of authorial freedom or agency become complicated through the workings of romantic irony? What is the relationship of author to hero? How are we to understand the introduction of the romantic (Byronic) hero on Russian soil? What is a reader? How are we to understand the interpretive and psychological processes by which a reader “identifies” with her prototype? What constitutes a legitimate reading? What is commentary? What is genre? How to understand the prosaic and the poetic in themselves, and as fused aspects of a “novel in verse,” Pushkin’s paradoxical definition of <em>Evgenii Onegin</em>? What is the relationship of a text to literary history? How to understand such significant literary movements as sentimentalism and romanticism, their formal preoccupations and cultural assumptions? <em>Evgenii Onegin </em>has generated vastly divergent interpretations, from Belinskii, who viewed the novel as an “encyclopedia of Russian life,” to Nabokov’s insistence that the novel and its characters were a purely literary stylization of prior European and Russian sources. These divergences point to two broadly distinct approaches to scholarship, one pointing in the direction of <em>historicism</em>, the other to various <em>formalisms and structuralisms.</em> Over the course of the semester we will be examining both historicist and formalist approaches to the text, those arising from Pushkin criticism as well as from much further afield. We will be exploring the largely thematic and socio-historical approaches typical of nineteenth-century Russian criticism; at the same time we will be asking if 20th-century scholars of the novel, such as Bakhtin, or of cultural history, such as Lotman, might serve to refine the assumptions of 19th-century criticism. At the same time we will be examining the stylistic specificities of the text, focusing on such questions as the correlation of rhythm and syntax or the patterning of the Onegin stanza. Can we usefully compare Russian and Western approaches to genre theory, cultural history, intertextuality, romantic irony, the unconscious, gender and sexuality?</p>
<p><strong>Requirements: </strong>Students are expected to have purchased and already read <em>Evgenii Onegin</em> prior to the commencement of the semester.  All other readings are to be provided online on bCourses. Make sure to bring a copy of the novel as well as the week’s readings (as a hard copy or on your computer) every week.</p>
<p>The final grade will be based on:</p>
<ol>
<li>your brief (10-15 minute) oral reports (sign up for at least two such reports during the second week’s class) as well as your participation in class discussion</li>
<li>nine tightly argued 1-2 page written assignments due when indicated in the syllabus</li>
<li>A paper proposal (due November 28) and a 8-12 page term paper (due December 9). Students may also opt for a take-home exam in place of a final paper.</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>Required text:</strong><br />
A.S. Pushkin, <em>Eugene Onegin</em>, A.D. Briggs (editor). Duckworth Press.<br />
<strong>ISBN-13:</strong> 978-1853993961</p>
<p><strong>Prerequisite</strong>:  Graduate standing.</p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-2099 -->

<article id="post-2100" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Slavic 280, Section 2: Graduate Seminar: Facts and Fictions: Factographic Strategies in Russian Prose</h1>
		<p class="section-spacing">
																Tu 2-5, 6115 Dwinelle. <b>Instructor: Edward Tyerman</b>.
								
								</p>
							
									
							
							
														
						
															<p class="ls-breadth"><span class="units">Units: 4</span>
										</p>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">

	
		<p>This course explores the discourse of “fact” within Russian literary culture. We will focus on movements and moments in Russian literary history that have affirmed the value of texts based on documents and eyewitness experience over the truth claims of fiction. In theoretical terms, the course will involve a historical exploration of the ways in which the lines between fact and fiction, <em>dokumental’naia proza </em>and <em>khudozhestvennaia proza</em>, have been drawn.</p>
<p>Central to our concerns will be the “literature of fact” movement of the 1920s, a development within the left avant-garde that insisted on the abolition of fiction and the distribution of texts based on socially useful factual information. We will situate the factographers within contemporary debates on the form and function of revolutionary literature, comparing factographic theory and practice (Tret’iakov, Chuzhak, Brik, Kushner) with the work of other contemporary writers who interrogate the line between fact and fiction (Babel, Pil’niak, Kataev, Shklovskii). Contemporaneous debates on “played” vs “unplayed” cinema will also merit our attention (Eisenstein, Vertov, Kalatozov), as will theorizations of the status and role of photography. In addition, the course will trace a pre-history of factography in the <em>ocherki</em> of the 19<sup>th</sup> century, and examine the afterlives of factography in postwar documentary prose (Solzhenitsyn, Adamovich, Alexievich).</p>
<p>Alongside recent work on documentary writing in the Slavic field (Fore, Papazian, Toker), theoretical readings will offer a set of conceptual frameworks for thinking about the distinctions between fact and fiction and the role of documentary writing within a literary system (Tret’iakov, Chuzhak, Brik, Shklovskii, Tynianov, Lukacs, Ginzburg, Genette, Barthes, Derrida, Lejeune).</p>
<p><strong>Prerequisites:</strong>  Graduate standing; consent of instructor.</p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-2100 -->

			
		
		</div><!-- #content-container -->

<article id="post-2102" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Slavic 310: Internship in the Teaching of Literature/Linguistics</h1>
		<p class="section-spacing">
																Day/Time TBA, Location TBA. <b>Instructor: Instructor TBA</b>.
								
								</p>
							
									
							
							
														
						
															<p class="ls-breadth"><span class="units">Units: 2</span>
										</p>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">

	
		<p>Weekly meetings with the instructor of the designated course.  Discussion of course aims, syllabus preparation, lecture and assignment planning, grading and related matters.  Students may prepare a representative portion of the work for such a course (e.g. lecture outline and assignments for a course segment) and may participate in presentation of the material and in evaluation of samples of student work.  May be repeated for credit.</p>
<p><strong> Prerequisites:</strong>  Slavic graduate student status and consent of instructor.</p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-2102 -->

<article id="post-2090" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Slavic 50: Introduction to Russian/East European/Eurasian Cultures</h1>
		<p class="section-spacing">
																TuTh 9:30-11, 102 Wheeler. <b>Instructor: Darya Kavitskaya</b>.
								
								</p>
							
									
							
							
														
						
															<p class="ls-breadth"><span class="units">Units: 3</span>
					 <em>Satisfies L&S Arts & Literature OR Historical Studies OR Social & Behavioral Sciences breadth requirement.</em>					</p>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">

	
		<p><strong>Slavic 50 is required for the major track in Russian/East European/Eurasian Cultures. </strong></p>
<p>This course is designed to familiarize the student with the major topics and events of Slavic cultural history, from their pre-historic origins to 21st century issues of identity and socio-politics. Readings will come from a variety of sources including historical chronicles and saints’ lives, fairy tales, poetry, short stories, excerpts from novels, mass media and scholarly articles. In addition, three subtitled films highlighting different aspects and eras of Slavic culture will be screened during the semester.</p>
<p>Class discussion and all readings will be in English. Students with no prior background in Slavic language, literature, or history are welcome and encouraged to enroll.</p>
<p><strong>Texts</strong>: to be published at bCourses.</p>
<p><strong>Prerequisites:</strong> None.</p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-2090 -->

<article id="post-2092" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Slavic 131: Literature, Art, and Society in 20th-Century Russia: Literature and Revolution</h1>
		<p class="section-spacing">
																TuTh 11-12:30, 88 Dwinelle. <b>Instructor: Harsha Ram</b>.
								
								</p>
							
									
							
							
														
						
															<p class="ls-breadth"><span class="units">Units: 4</span>
					 <em>Satisfies L&S Arts & Literature breadth requirement.</em>					</p>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">

	
		<p><strong>Cross-listed with Comparative Literature 155, Section 1</strong></p>
<p>The 100th anniversary of the Russian revolution seems an ideal moment to go back and examine the history and literature of revolutionary Russia. This was an era of violent upheaval, material destitution, and radical projections of social renewal and human transformation. We will be tracing the arc of the first revolutionary decade, from the revolutionary upsurge of 1917 and the Civil War of 1918-1921, which saw the ultimate consolidation of Soviet power, to the early and often contradictory formulations of Soviet culture arising over the course of the 1920s. Our course will focus primarily on the relationship between literature and revolution, a vital question given the importance paid to written culture by Bolsheviks and non-Bolsheviks alike. How did the writer bear witness to the first socialist revolution in history? Is revolution an event or a process, and how might it be narrated? Is literature a mirror of history or can it also serve actively to shape change? How did the principle literary genres &#8212; poetry, drama, the short story and the novel &#8212; serve the goal of imaging the revolution? How did political and literary theory animate the debates of the time? Was there room for satire and laughter in a socialist society? If the 20th century can be deemed the era of utopia and dystopia, what does Russian literature teach us about the hopes and failures of revolutionary transformation?</p>
<p>Writers and thinkers we will be reading include John Reed and Victor Serge, Karl Marx and Vladimir Lenin, Vladimir Mayakovsky and Velimir Khlebnikov, Isaak Babel and Boris Pilnyak, Viktor Shklovsky and Leon Trotsky, Mikhail Zoshchenko and Yuri Olesha, Evgeny Zamyatin and Andrei Platonov. In addition to a course reader, you will be required to purchase the following books.</p>
<p><strong>BOOKS TO BE PURCHASED</strong></p>
<p>John Reed, <em>Ten Days That Shook the World </em>(Penguin Classics) 978-0-140-18293-4</p>
<p>Vladimir Mayakovsky,<em> Selected Poems </em>(Northwestern World Classics) 978-0-8101-2907-8</p>
<p>Isaak Babel, <em>Red Cavalry (</em>Norton) 978-0-393-32423-5</p>
<p>Boris Pilnyak,<em> The Naked Year </em>(Overlook Press) 978-1-4683-0639-2</p>
<p>Yuri Olesha,<em> Envy </em>(New York Review Books<em>) </em>1-59017-086-5</p>
<p>Evgeny Zamyatin, <em>We </em>(Penguin) 0-14-018585-2</p>
<p>Andrey Platonov,<em> Soul: And Other Stories </em>(New York Review Books<em>) </em>978-1-59017-254-4</p>
<p><strong>Prerequisites:</strong>  none.</p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-2092 -->

<article id="post-2088" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Slavic 45: Nineteenth-Century Russian Literature</h1>
		<p class="section-spacing">
																MWF 12-1, 182 Dwinelle. <b>Instructor: Luba Golburt</b>.
								
								</p>
							
									
							
							
														
						
															<p class="ls-breadth"><span class="units">Units: 4</span>
					 <em>Satisfies L&S Arts & Literature breadth requirement.</em>					</p>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">

	
		<p>We will read some of the celebrated works of the Russian nineteenth-century, from Pushkin through Lermontov, Gogol, Turgenev, Dostoevsky and Tolstoy to Chekhov.</p>
<p>It is the nineteenth century that poses the decisive questions of Russian modernity: the quest for an identity for the nation and its language, the problem of literary realism, the responsibilities of literature as an agent of social change, the status of the writer within changing social hierarchies, the correlation of the personal and the collective, the political and the metaphysical. These are decades of formal exploration and cultural anxiety, existential doubt and political denunciation. To define the nineteenth century and its questions and make them speak to us will be our goal this semester.</p>
<p>The course is a prerequisite for admission to the Slavic major and is recommended for prospective graduate students in Slavic. No knowledge of Russian is required; the classes are conducted in English.</p>
<p><strong>Texts:</strong></p>
<p>Pushkin, <em>Eugene Onegin</em>, trans. James E. Falen, Oxford UP; ISBN: 0192838995</p>
<p>Lermontov. <em>A Hero of Our Time</em><strong>,</strong> trans. Paul Foote, Penguin Classics; ISBN: 0140447958</p>
<p>Gogol. <em>The Collected Tales of Nikolai Gogol</em>, trans. Richard Pevear, Larissa Volokhonsky; Vintage Classics; ISBN: 0375706158</p>
<p>Turgenev. <em>Fathers and Children</em><strong>.</strong> Trans. Michael R. Katz, Norton Critical Edition; ISBN: 0393927979</p>
<p>Dostoevsky. <em>Notes from Underground</em><strong>,</strong> Ed. and Trans. Michael Katz, Norton Critical Edition; ISBN: 0393976120</p>
<p>Tolstoy, <em>The Cossacks and Other Stories</em>, Trans. David McDuff, Penguin Classics; ISBN: 0140449590</p>
<p>Tolstoy, <em>Childhood, Boyhood, Youth</em>. Trans. Judson Rosengrant, Penguin Classics; ISBN: 0140449922</p>
<p>Chekhov. <em>The Portable Chekhov</em><strong>.</strong> Edited by Avrahm Yarmolinsky, Viking Portable Library; ISBN: 0140150353</p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-2088 -->

			
		
		</div><!-- #content-container -->

<li id="menu-item-1588" class="menu-item menu-item-type-post_type menu-item-object-page menu-item-1588"><a href="http://slavic.berkeley.edu/courses-2/complete-list-of-department-courses/">Complete List of Department Courses</a></li>

<article id="post-2101" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Slavic 281: Proseminar: Evgenii Onegin</h1>
		<p class="section-spacing">
																Th 3-6, 6115 Dwinelle. <b>Instructor: Harsha Ram</b>.
								
								</p>
							
									
							
							
														
						
															<p class="ls-breadth"><span class="units">Units: 4</span>
										</p>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">

	
		<p>This seminar seeks to acquaint students with various approaches to literary scholarship. Numerous methodologies, both Russian and Western, will be introduced and tested with respect to the questions they inherently pose and with respect to their applicability to Pushkin’s <em>Evgenii Onegin</em>, the primary text we will be working with over the course of the semester. We shall be asking such questions as: what is a text, philologically and theoretically speaking? What, if any, are its boundaries? What is the relationship between text, intertext and context? What is an author? How does the classic notion of authorial freedom or agency become complicated through the workings of romantic irony? What is the relationship of author to hero? How are we to understand the introduction of the romantic (Byronic) hero on Russian soil? What is a reader? How are we to understand the interpretive and psychological processes by which a reader “identifies” with her prototype? What constitutes a legitimate reading? What is commentary? What is genre? How to understand the prosaic and the poetic in themselves, and as fused aspects of a “novel in verse,” Pushkin’s paradoxical definition of <em>Evgenii Onegin</em>? What is the relationship of a text to literary history? How to understand such significant literary movements as sentimentalism and romanticism, their formal preoccupations and cultural assumptions? <em>Evgenii Onegin </em>has generated vastly divergent interpretations, from Belinskii, who viewed the novel as an “encyclopedia of Russian life,” to Nabokov’s insistence that the novel and its characters were a purely literary stylization of prior European and Russian sources. These divergences point to two broadly distinct approaches to scholarship, one pointing in the direction of <em>historicism</em>, the other to various <em>formalisms and structuralisms.</em> Over the course of the semester we will be examining both historicist and formalist approaches to the text, those arising from Pushkin criticism as well as from much further afield. We will be exploring the largely thematic and socio-historical approaches typical of nineteenth-century Russian criticism; at the same time we will be asking if 20th-century scholars of the novel, such as Bakhtin, or of cultural history, such as Lotman, might serve to refine the assumptions of 19th-century criticism. At the same time we will be examining the stylistic specificities of the text, focusing on such questions as the correlation of rhythm and syntax or the patterning of the Onegin stanza. Can we usefully compare Russian and Western approaches to genre theory, cultural history, intertextuality, romantic irony, the unconscious, gender and sexuality?</p>
<p><strong>Requirements: </strong>Students are expected to have purchased and already read <em>Evgenii Onegin</em> prior to the commencement of the semester.  All other readings are to be provided online on bCourses. Make sure to bring a copy of the novel as well as the week’s readings (as a hard copy or on your computer) every week.</p>
<p>The final grade will be based on:</p>
<ol>
<li>your brief (10-15 minute) oral reports (sign up for at least two such reports during the second week’s class) as well as your participation in class discussion</li>
<li>nine tightly argued 1-2 page written assignments due when indicated in the syllabus</li>
<li>A paper proposal (due November 28) and a 8-12 page term paper (due December 9). Students may also opt for a take-home exam in place of a final paper.</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>Required text:</strong><br />
A.S. Pushkin, <em>Eugene Onegin</em>, A.D. Briggs (editor). Duckworth Press.<br />
<strong>ISBN-13:</strong> 978-1853993961</p>
<p><strong>Prerequisite</strong>:  Graduate standing.</p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-2101 -->

<article id="post-2083" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Slavic R5A, Section 3: The Real and the Grotesque: Russian Literature and the American South</h1>
		<p class="section-spacing">
																MWF 8-9, 246 Dwinelle. <b>Instructor: Kit Pribble</b>.
								
								</p>
							
									
							
							
														
						
															<p class="ls-breadth"><span class="units">Units: 4</span>
										</p>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">

	
		<p>This course satisfies the first half or the “A” portion of the Reading and Composition requirement.</p>
<p>Despite the geographical and temporal distances separating them, Russian literature of the 19<sup>th</sup> century has been productively linked to the Southern Gothic mode in 20<sup>th</sup>-century American fiction. Common to both traditions is a preoccupation with the demonic side of everyday life—largely seen as resulting from inherently unequal socioeconomic systems—and a corresponding tendency to blur the line between “realistic” and “grotesque” description. In this course, we will interrogate the tension between avowedly realist representations of daily life and human psychology, and those representations that have been labeled Gothic or grotesque. We will read selected texts from Russian writers including Nikolai Gogol, Ivan Goncharov, Ivan Turgenev, and Fyodor Dostoevsky, alongside texts by Southern writers that may include William Faulkner, Flannery O’Connor, and Carson McCullers, among others. We may also consider the Southern Gothic mode in other types of contemporary media, including the television series <em>True Detective</em> and <em>American Horror Story</em>.</p>
<p>Over the course of the semester we will attempt to answer the following questions: How do we define the category of the “grotesque” in contradistinction to the “real”? Is the grotesque capable of conveying moral and psychological truths in a way that is inaccessible to more traditional forms of realistic description? What are the sociopolitical conditions that give rise to grotesque art? And finally, how do these conditions manifest themselves in both 19<sup>th</sup>-century Russia and 20<sup>th</sup>-century American South, so that Southern modernists frequently drew inspiration from the Russian realist tradition?</p>
<p>While these questions will guide the readings and discussions that form the structural centerpiece of this course, we will also focus on developing the skills of college-level critical reading and writing. In this course students will outline, draft, write, and rewrite a series of essays, honing their ability to read closely and craft persuasive written arguments.</p>
<p><strong>While the majority of readings will be provided either online or in a course reader, students should purchase the following texts: </strong></p>
<p>Nikolai Gogol. <em>Dead Souls</em>. Trans. Richard Pevear and Larissa Volokhonsky. Vintage, 1997. [ISBN-10: 0679776443; ISBN-13: 9780679776444]</p>
<p>Fyodor Dostoevsky. <em>The Double and The Gambler</em>. Trans. Richard Pevear and Larissa Volokhonsky. Vintage, 2007. [ISBN-10: 0375719016; ISBN-13: 9780375719011]</p>
<p>William Faulkner. <em>Selected Short Stories</em>. Modern Library, 1993. [ISBN-10: 0679424784; ISBN-13: 9780679424789]</p>
<p><strong>Prerequisite:</strong>  Successful completion of the UC Entry Level Writing Requirement.  Students may not enroll in nor attend R1A/R5A courses without completing this prerequisite.</p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-2083 -->

<article id="post-2084" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Slavic R5B, Section 1: Belief and Rebellion in the Modern World: Examining the Ethics of Resistance in Russian Literature</h1>
		<p class="section-spacing">
																TuTh 8-9:30, 2030 VLSB. <b>Instructor: Jennifer Flaherty</b>.
								
								</p>
							
									
							
							
														
						
															<p class="ls-breadth"><span class="units">Units: 4</span>
										</p>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">

	
		<p><strong>This course satisfies the second half or the &#8220;B&#8221; portion of the Reading and Composition requirement. </strong></p>
<p>How much oppression can a person take without fighting back?</p>
<p>What are the ethics of not following the rules?</p>
<p>In Dostoevsky’s <em>Crime and Punishment</em>, the hero murders an exploitative pawnbroker on behalf of social justice. In the works of Nikolai Gogol, characters undergo the experience of being treated as nobodies: underprivileged, with nothing to offer to a ruthless, competitive world. Anton Chekhov’s short stories convey the crushing despair of guilt in the face of other people’s oppression and the seeming pointlessness of action in a world so full of injustice for so many people.</p>
<p>The goal of this class will be to address the question of action in the face of social injustice. We’ll look at this question through the study of Russian literature, but we’ll also think about ways to connect the issues raised in the texts we read to the pressing issues of our current time and space. In particular, by investigating the large gap between the rich and the poor in Russia in the mid- to late 1800s, we will engage the broader question of oppression and resistance.</p>
<p>As this course is dedicated to developing the skills of college-level writing, we will focus on expanding and expressing our ideas through writing. A key aim will be to learn techniques that help us organize complex ideas into argumentation, and to develop our own writerly voice.</p>
<p>In contemporary literature from across the globe, we continue to see the influence of Russian writers, whose themes we will use to reflect the struggles of our own times. In particular, we will read from the work one American author, Ralph Ellison, and one contemporary Indian author, Aravind Adiga, to see how Dostoevsky’s themes in particular can be used to think about American racism and contemporary classism. As we will see, readers of Russian literature are given the opportunity to ponder deep questions about the experience of oppression—both our own and other people’s— and to consider the variety of human responses to that oppression.</p>
<p><strong>Students should purchase:</strong></p>
<p><strong> </strong>(<em>Note: most readings will be compiled into a course reader</em>)</p>
<p>Aravind Adiga, <em>The White Tiger: A Novel</em>, ISBN: 1416562591</p>
<p>Fyodor Dostoevsky, <em>Crime and Punishment</em> (trans. Pevear and Volokhonsky), ISBN: 9780679734505</p>
<p><strong>Prerequisite:</strong>  Successful completion of the “A” portion of the Reading &amp; Composition requirement or its equivalent.  <strong>Students may not enroll in nor attend R1B/R5B courses without completing this prerequisite.</strong></p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-2084 -->

<article id="post-2085" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Slavic R5B, Section 2: There Once Lived a Woman: Women Writers Adapt the Fairy Tale</h1>
		<p class="section-spacing">
																TuTh 8-9:30, 2070 VLSB. <b>Instructor: Karina McCorkle</b>.
								
								</p>
							
									
							
							
														
						
															<p class="ls-breadth"><span class="units">Units: 4</span>
										</p>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">

	
		<p><strong>This course fulfills the second half, or “B” portion, of the Reading and Composition requirement.</strong></p>
<p>Fairy tales tend to be a fraught place for women. If they are not witches, evil queens, stepmothers, or stepsisters, then they are often orphans, endure terrible abuse, or in a best case scenario, are simply prizes to be given away to a successful male hero right before the “happily ever after.” In twentieth century literature, many women writers have attempted to bring the fairy tale back under female control by rewriting it. These are texts in which gender roles are reversed, folklore logic is thrown into question, or women characters from classic fairy tales get to tell their side of the story. Although we will primarily focus on Slavic fairy tales and Slavic women writers, especially the short stories of Tatyana Tolstaya and Lyudmilla Petrushevskaya, we will also examine the work of several American women writers and poets, like Shirley Jackson and Anne Sexton. During this course, we will attempt to answer questions like: What is the fairy tale definition of womanhood? What happens when fairy tale types are put into a realistic setting? How does our understanding of a realistic story change when it is told like a fairy tale? How is a fairy tale different when it is told from a woman’s perspective? Together we will look at how each of these writers adapts the raw material of fairy tales, and see what that teaches us about both the fairy tales themselves and the individual writer’s goals.</p>
<p>The primary goal of this class is to teach college level writing and research skills. In addition to learning how to make clear, persuasive arguments in papers, we will look at both how to find outside scholarship and how it can be incorporated to support such arguments.</p>
<p><strong>Most texts will be provided either online or in a course reader—however, the following novels should be purchased:</strong></p>
<p><strong> </strong><em>House of Day, House of Night</em>, Olga Tokarczuk (ISBN: 0810118920)</p>
<p><em>Baba Yaga Laid An Egg, </em>Dubravka Ugresič (ISBN: 0802145205)</p>
<p><strong>Prerequisite:</strong>  Successful completion of the “A” portion of the Reading &amp; Composition requirement or its equivalent.  <strong>Students may not enroll in nor attend R1B/R5B courses without completing this prerequisite.</strong></p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-2085 -->

<article id="post-2086" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Slavic R5B, Section 3: Photography and Narration</h1>
		<p class="section-spacing">
																TuTh 8-9:30, 104 Barrows. <b>Instructor: Robyn Jensen</b>.
								
								</p>
							
									
							
							
														
						
															<p class="ls-breadth"><span class="units">Units: 4</span>
										</p>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">

	
		<p><strong>This course satisfies the second half or the &#8220;B&#8221; portion of the Reading and Composition requirement. </strong></p>
<p>What can a photograph tell us? From its inception, photography has been hailed as an exemplary method for objectively capturing reality. And yet, photographs can be manipulated in numerous ways, from removing images from their original context to using a “nostalgic” filter on Instagram. This course looks at how writers have reflected on and incorporated photography into their works. We will explore what has fascinated writers and thinkers about photography as a medium. As Susan Sontag writes, photographs “are inexhaustible invitations to deduction, speculation, and fantasy.”</p>
<p>Throughout the course, the relationship between reality and imagination, history and memory, fact and fiction will demand our attention. As we read, some of the questions we will consider are: What is the relationship between word and image? How do writers use the photograph album to narrate? Do photographs verify or deceive? What would it mean to narrate photographically?</p>
<p>We will explore a range of genres that engage with photography and its narrative potentials, including: poems by Vladimir Mayakovsky; short stories by Anton Chekhov, Mikhail Zoshchenko, Andrei Bitov, Dubravka Ugrešić, and W.G. Sebald; essays by Ilya Ehrenburg and Joseph Brodsky; a memoir by Vladimir Nabokov; films by Dziga Vertov, Alfred Hitchcock, Michelangelo Antonioni, and Chris Marker; as well as a theoretical text on photography by Roland Barthes.</p>
<p>The primary goal of this course is to develop our reading and writing skills. Students will be guided through the various stages of writing, such as outlining, drafting, writing, and revising. Students will also gain experience finding, evaluating, and incorporating secondary sources to support their arguments.</p>
<p><strong>Most texts will be available either online or in a course reader. Students should purchase the following texts:</strong></p>
<p>Roland Barthes, <em>Camera Lucida: Reflections on Photography</em>, trans. Richard Howard (New York: Hill and Wang, 2010). ISBN: 978-0374532338</p>
<p>Vladimir Nabokov, <em>Speak, Memory: An Autobiography Revisited </em>(New York: Vintage, 1989). ISBN: 978-0679723394</p>
<p><strong>Prerequisite:</strong>  Successful completion of the “A” portion of the Reading &amp; Composition requirement or its equivalent.  <strong>Students may not enroll in nor attend R1B/R5B courses without completing this prerequisite.</strong></p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

<article id="post-2081" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Slavic R5A, Section 1: Writing (Against) War: Representing and Resisting Conflict in Literature and Film</h1>
		<p class="section-spacing">
																MWF 8-9, 228 Dwinelle. <b>Instructor: Antje Postema</b>.
								
								</p>
							
									
							
							
														
						
															<p class="ls-breadth"><span class="units">Units: 4</span>
										</p>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">

	
		<p>This course satisfies the first half or the “A” portion of the Reading and Composition requirement.</p>
<p>This course focuses on literary texts and films that significantly engage with historical conflicts in the modern period, with a specific focus on the Slavic speaking world. We will examine literary and filmic representations of historical warfare, keeping in mind the temporal and geographical context of each work. The majority of our attention will be on the variety of generic and rhetorical positions authors take as they represent war. We will analyze the seeming paradox at the root of artistic representation of conflict: that the upheaval of war seems both to demand representation and to inhibit it.</p>
<p>Out of this paradox emerge many of the course’s major questions: What characteristic genres or tropes recur in these texts, though they may be written decades apart and in different regions of the globe? Do war stories differ when they are told from the perspective of civilians or soldiers, women or men, victims or perpetrators? How do particular media shape stories? What role do “truth” or “accuracy” play in navigating between lived experience and artistic representation? Is the changing face of war across the long 20<sup>th</sup> century matched by changes in its treatment in literature and film? Are there, after all, unified genres or modes that might legitimately be termed “war literature” or “war film”?</p>
<p>This class will provide opportunities to develop three interrelated skills: critical reading, meaningful discussion, and clear academic writing. By the end of the semester, students will be comfortable analyzing a variety of texts with a careful eye for nuance and considering alternative viewpoints. Reading will be approximately 60-80 pages per week. Students will be expected to complete all assigned reading and come to class prepared to participate actively in class discussion. Writing assignments will include close readings of specific texts and films, two analytical papers, and a final research paper. Over the course of the semester, students will focus on all stages of the writing process: selecting an essay topic, crafting a thesis, developing and sustaining an argument, structuring units of prose, editing and proofreading, word choice, sentence flow, and other topics in grammar, mechanics, and usage.</p>
<p>Required Texts:</p>
<ol>
<li>Svetlana Alexievich, <em>Zinky Boys: Soviet Voices From a Forgotten War</em>. New York: W.W. Norton, 1992. ISBN: 0393336867</li>
<li>Tadeusz Borowski, <em>This Way for the Gas, Ladies and Gentlemen</em>. Penguin Classics, 1992. ISBN: 0140186247.</li>
<li>Andrei Gelasimov. Las Vegas, NV: AmazonCrossing, 2011. ISBN: 1611090695</li>
</ol>
<ol>
<li>Miljenko Jergović. <em>Sarajevo Marlboro.</em> Brooklyn, NY: Archipelago, 2003. ISBN: 0972869220</li>
</ol>
<ol start="5">
<li>Kurt Vonnegut. <em>Slaughterhouse-Five.</em> New York: Dial Press, 2009. ISBN: 0385333846</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>Prerequisite</strong>:  Successful completion of the “A” portion of the Reading &amp; Composition requirement or its equivalent.  Students may not enroll in nor attend R1B/R5B courses without completing this prerequisite.</p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-2081 -->

<article id="post-2082" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Slavic R5A, Section 2: Literature for Scientists</h1>
		<p class="section-spacing">
																TuTh 8-9:30, 2011 VLSB. <b>Instructor: Brian Egdorf</b>.
								
								</p>
							
									
							
							
														
						
															<p class="ls-breadth"><span class="units">Units: 4</span>
										</p>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">

	
		<p>This course satisfies the first half or the “A” portion of the Reading and Composition requirement.</p>
<p>What can a scientist gain from reading literature? Using this as our guiding question, we will investigate several examples of Russian and English literature from the nineteenth century in which the “literary” and the “scientific” collide. We will encounter botched experiments, scientists as characters, dangerous scientific ideas, evolution, scientific expedition and travel, patients, doctors, dissection of frogs, early psychology and neuroscience, social experimentation, and more. We will take a look more broadly at how scientific ideas are absorbed into literature, sometimes with unexpected results. What role does literature play in representing the complexities of science in the real world? What is the risk of applying scientific ideas to society and culture? We will attempt to trace the lineage of debates around the role of science in society and culture, starting in the eighteenth and nineteenth centuries and concluding in the present.</p>
<p>This course is primarily intended to develop college writing and critical reading skills. Over the course of the semester, students will write expository essays and practice developing strong arguments through close reading.</p>
<p><strong>Texts:</strong></p>
<p>Texts for purchase (Important: You <em>must</em> have these paper Norton editions; others are unacceptable).</p>
<p>Mary Shelley, <em>Frankenstein, </em>ISBN: 0393927938 (Norton Critical Edition)</p>
<p>Ivan Turgenev, <em>Fathers and Children, </em>ISBN: 0393927970 (Norton Critical Edition)</p>
<p>Fyodor Dostoevsky, <em>Notes from the Underground, </em>ISBN: 0393976122 (Norton Critical Edition)</p>
<p>Joseph Conrad, <em>Heart of Darkness, </em>ISBN: 0393926362 (Norton Critical Edition)</p>
<p><em> (Supplementary readings will be posted on bCourses)</em></p>
<p><strong>Prerequisite:</strong>  Successful completion of the UC Entry Level Writing Requirement.  Students may not enroll in nor attend R1A/R5A courses without completing this prerequisite.</p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-2082 -->

<article id="post-2231" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Slavic 181: Readings in Russian Literature</h1>
		<p class="section-spacing">
																TuTh 4-5:30, 6307 Dwinelle. <b>Instructor: Olga Matich</b>.
								
								</p>
							
									
							
							
														
						
															<p class="ls-breadth"><span class="units">Units: 4</span>
					 <em>Satisfies L&S Arts & Literature breadth requirement.</em>					</p>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">

	
		<p>Study and analysis of the development of the Russian literary language and short fiction from the eighteenth century to the present.</p>
<p>Taught in Russian.<br />
<strong>Prerequisites</strong>: Russian 103A (which may be taken concurrently) or language placement assessment.</p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-2231 -->

			
		
		</div><!-- #content-container -->



	</div><!-- #main -->

	<footer id="footer" class="wideness" role="contentinfo">

<article id="post-2091" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Slavic 100: Seminar: Russian, East European, and Eurasian Cultures</h1>
		<p class="section-spacing">
																W 1-3, 6115 Dwinelle. <b>Instructor: Darya Kavitskaya</b>.
								
								</p>
							
									
							
							
														
						
															<p class="ls-breadth"><span class="units">Units: 3</span>
					 <em>Satisfies L&S Arts & Literature OR Historical Studies OR Social & Behavioral Sciences breadth requirement.</em>					</p>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">

	
		<p>In this seminar, students will undertake an in-depth exploration of various aspects of cultural history, literature, language, and society of Eastern Europe and the former Soviet Union. Aimed at introducing students to the diverse field of Russian and East European studies, the course will feature a number of guest speakers—scholars who will provide introductions to the various areas and present their own research. Course readings include diverse primary texts (literature, popular culture, journalism, etc.) and representative scholarly studies. Films and media materials will be shown and discussed in the course of the semester. Taught in a seminar format, this course makes a special emphasis on research. Participants will receive an opportunity to produce a research paper (10-20 pages) on the topic of their interests, working in individual consultations with both the primary instructor and other faculty members. (Students working on a senior or honors thesis may find this seminar useful for their thesis research.)</p>
<p>Required of all majors in the Department of Slavic Languages and Literatures, in Fall 2017 this course is also open to other students who are interested in research on this vital area of the world.</p>
<p><strong>Prerequisite:</strong>  Major status in the Department of Slavic Languages and Literatures or the consent of the instructor.</p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-2091 -->

			
		
		</div><!-- #content-container -->

<article id="post-1947" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Slavic R5B, Section 101: Session D (July 3- August 11): Mapping Spaces, Mapping Texts</h1>
		<p class="section-spacing">
																TuWTh 3-5:30, 187 Dwinelle. <b>Instructor: Isobel Palmer</b>.
								
								</p>
							
									
							
							
														
						
															<p class="ls-breadth"><span class="units">Units: 4</span>
										</p>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">

	
		<p><strong>All Reading and Composition courses must be taken for a letter grade in order to fulfill this requirement for the Bachelor’s Degree. This course satisfies the second half or the “B” portion of the Reading and Composition requirement.</strong></p>
<p>This is a course about modern urban space—a central feature of our daily experience and identities, but rarely something we stop to think about. This course has been designed to give us an opportunity to do just that: to pause and think about the ways in which urban landscape structures our social and mental life, our habits and our time. What is the relationship between the physical city and the city of social interaction, memory, fantasy or desire—between urban and imaginary space? How do we experience space and relate to other people as we move through cities? And what kind of spaces do we occupy when we read about cities, or when we write about them?</p>
<p>The course begins in San Francisco, with Rebecca Solnit’s recent atlas, <em>Infinite City. </em>From unusual representations of an urban space we all know well, we’ll then move through a variety of cities around the globe. We’ll follow <em>Crime and Punishment’</em>s murderous Raskolnikov around St. Petersburg, take a bath in the Parisian crowd with Charles Baudelaire, experience life on a barge with Penelope Fitzgerald in post-war London, and look at early Soviet Moscow through Iury Olesha’s upside-down binoculars. As we learn how to read these texts critically and to write about them as scholars, we’ll also experiment creatively to think about how we ourselves might choose to represent the spaces we live in and our experience of urban life.</p>
<p><em> </em>Navigating through different centuries, genders, and social classes, novels, poems and films, this course offers a chance to reflect on the construction of urban space and the diverse nature of our experiences, in San Francisco and beyond.</p>
<p><strong>Primary texts: </strong></p>
<p><strong>1. </strong><strong>Maps/cities</strong><br />
Rebecca Solnit. <em>Infinite City.</em><br />
Italo Calvino. <em>Invisible Cities.</em><br />
Elizabeth Bishop. “The Map”.</p>
<p><strong>2. Street/crowd</strong><br />
Nikolai Gogol, “Nevsky Prospect”<br />
Edgar Allen Poe, “A Man of the Crowd”<br />
Fyodor Dostoevsky, <em>Crime and Punishment </em>(selections)<em>,</em><br />
Andrei Bely, <em>Petersburg </em>(selections)<br />
Virginia Woolf, “Street Haunting”<br />
Charles Baudelaire (selections)<br />
Vladimir Mayakovsky (selections)<br />
Aleksandr Blok (selections)<br />
Alexander Hackenschmied. <em>An Aimless Walk </em>(film)<br />
<em>The London Perambulator </em>(documentary)<br />
Dziga Vertov. <em>Man with a Movie Camera </em>(film)</p>
<p><strong>3. Everyday urban life<br />
</strong>Iury Olesha. <em>Envy.<br />
</em>Anna Akhmatova (selections).<br />
Penelope Fitzgerald. <em>Offshore</em><br />
Wim Wenders. <em>Alice in the Cities. </em></p>
<p><strong>Prerequisites:</strong>  Successful completion of the “A” portion of the Reading &amp; Composition requirement or its equivalent.  Students may not enroll in nor attend R1B/R5B courses without completing this prerequisite.</p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
