<article id="post-1945" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Slavic R5B, Section 2: Session C (June 19-August 11): True Crime Revisited: Stories of Deviance</h1>
		<p class="section-spacing">
																TuWTh 12-2, 283 Dwinelle. <b>Instructor: Matthew Kendall</b>.
								
								</p>
							
									
							
							
														
						
															<p class="ls-breadth"><span class="units">Units: 4</span>
										</p>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">

	
		<p><strong>All Reading &amp; Composition courses must be taken for a letter grade in order to fulfill this requirement for the Bachelor’s Degree. This course satisfies the first half or the “B” portion of the Reading and Composition requirement.</strong></p>
<p>Over only a few years, true crime narratives have infiltrated popular television and podcasts. But these new stories of crime have older roots that often go overlooked: this course will ask how novels and films from the 19th and 20th centuries represented the intersection of law and morality to give new perspectives on criminal activity. Many have argued that the treatment of crime shows us more about how a society organizes itself than the severity of the crime itself – our course will ask how various artistic forms treat misdeeds.</p>
<p>Many of the works on our syllabus are inspired by true stories, and we will navigate the waters of adaptation and the influence of historical reality on fictional narratives throughout our course. We will also trace the rise of early true crime documentary from fictional narratives that were fascinated with deviance.</p>
<p>The central goal of this course is to develop the skills necessary for writing clear, persuasive, and well-informed papers. Secondly, we aim to cultivate basic and effective research skills, and to learn how resources like Berkeley’s library help us implement these skills. One of Berkeley’s librarians will visit our class and show us how to make use of the library’s resources. Because our course is an accelerated summer session, students should expect to read over 60 pages per week, and to attend every class meeting – attendance is crucial to success. Class sessions are 2 hours each, and students are expected to participate actively during discussion.</p>
<p><strong>Featured Texts:</strong><br />
E.A. Poe, “The Tell-Tale Heart” and “The Cask of Amantillado”<br />
Dostoevsky, “A Meek One”<br />
Tolstoy, “The Kreutzer Sonata”<br />
Babel’, “Salt&#8221;<br />
Capote, <em>In Cold Blood </em></p>
<p>Elevator to the Gallows, dir. Louis Malle<br />
Psycho, dir. Alfred Hitchock<br />
Chinatown, dir. Roman Polanski<br />
The Thin Blue Line, dir. Errol Morris</p>
<p><strong>Prerequisite:  Successful completion of the “A” portion of the Reading and Composition requirement or its equivalent.  Students may not enroll nor attend R1B/R5B courses without completing this prerequisite.</strong></p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-1945 -->

<article id="post-1948" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Slavic R5B, Section 1: Session C (June 19-August 11): Jurassic World vs Jurassic Park: What is Science Fiction?</h1>
		<p class="section-spacing">
																TuWTh 2-4, 235 Dwinelle. <b>Instructor: Thomas Dyne</b>.
								
								</p>
							
									
							
							
														
						
															<p class="ls-breadth"><span class="units">Units: 4</span>
										</p>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">

	
		<p><strong>All Reading and Composition courses must be taken for a letter grade in order to fulfill this requirement for the Bachelor’s Degree. This course satisfies the second half or the “B” portion of the Reading and Composition requirement.</strong></p>
<p>2015’s <em>Jurassic World</em> ranks among the most financially successful films ever made, but is it science fiction? Reviews label it a “pure fantasy” “effects-driven” “spectacle”, but the original <em>Jurassic Park</em> – the <em>New York Times</em> bestseller written in 1989 by former Harvard medical student Michael Crichton – was praised for its terse, journalistic narrative and science-driven plot. This class will pose the question of its title – what is science fiction? – as a problem of genre, and will ask: where is the line between fantasy and reality in science fiction, when everything represented either on screen or on the page is speculative, fictional, or, in some cases, impossible? How can we <em>realistically</em> narrate or represent what isn’t real?</p>
<p>To answer these questions we will examine short stories and novels of the Russian, British, and American science fiction traditions, including selections from <em>Jurassic Park </em>(Michael Crichton), <em>The Martian<u> </u></em>(Andy Weir),<em> A Clockwork Orange </em>(Anthony Burgess), <em>We</em> (Yevgeny Zamyatin), and “Bloodchild” (Octavia Butler), as well as the films <em>2001: A Space Odyssey</em> (Stanley Kubrick), <em>Alien</em> (Ridley Scott), and the television series <em>The Twilight Zone </em>(Rod Serling). We will also read some texts concerning the theory of realism in both fiction and cinema.</p>
<p>In addition to discussing weekly readings, our goal will be to develop and improve students&#8217; ability to read critically and write clear, well-reasoned, articulate and persuasive papers. Over the course of the semester, students will learn how to move from an interesting question, to a compelling argument, to a successful paper. Students will be encouraged to develop a final research project based on a theoretical line of inquiry that interests them.</p>
<p><strong>Prerequisites:</strong>  Successful completion of the “A” portion of the Reading &amp; Composition requirement or its equivalent.  Students may not enroll in nor attend R1B/R5B courses without completing this prerequisite.</p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-1948 -->

<article id="post-2104" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Slavic 375B: Teaching Methods for Reading &#038; Composition</h1>
		<p class="section-spacing">
																TuTh 8-9:30/TBD, 6106 Dwinelle. <b>Instructor: Luba Golburt</b>.
								
								</p>
							
									
							
							
														
						
															<p class="ls-breadth"><span class="units">Units: 3</span>
										</p>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">

	
		<p><strong>This course is required of all Graduate Student Instructors teaching Reading &amp; Composition courses in the Slavic Department.</strong><strong><br />
</strong><br />
Course to be repeated for credit each semester of employment as Graduate Student Instructor.  The purpose of this course is to introduce new GSIs to teaching Slavic R5A and R5B.  It will focus on preparation of teaching materials, including syllabi, and discussion of questions of pedagogy (teaching literature and writing, lecturing, leading class discussions, designing writing assignments, grading and formulating responses to student papers, working with students individually and in small groups).  The course will help you prepare for a career as a college teacher of literature and for the teaching component of job applications.  Must be taken on a satisfactory/unsatisfactory basis.</p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-2104 -->

			
		
		</div><!-- #content-container -->



	</div><!-- #main -->

<article id="post-2103" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Slavic 375A: Teaching Methods for Slavic Languages: Russian 1-4 GSIs</h1>
		<p class="section-spacing">
																W 12-2, 6210 Dwinelle. <b>Instructor: Anna Muza</b>.
								
								</p>
							
									
							
							
														
						
															<p class="ls-breadth"><span class="units">Units: 3</span>
										</p>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">

	
		<p><strong>This course is required of all Graduate Student Instructors of Russian 1-4.</strong></p>
<p>Course to be repeated for credit each semester of employment as graduate student instructor. Course on practical teaching methods, grading, testing, and design of supplementary course materials.  Must be taken on a satisfactory/unsatisfactory basis.</p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

<article id="post-2093" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Slavic 134D: The Works of Leo Tolstoy</h1>
		<p class="section-spacing">
																TuTh 5-6:30, 130 Dwinelle. <b>Instructor: Eric Naiman</b>.
								
								</p>
							
									
							
							
														
						
															<p class="ls-breadth"><span class="units">Units: 4</span>
					 <em>Satisfies L&S Philosophy & Values OR Arts & Literature breadth requirement.</em>					</p>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">

	
		<p>Most of the semester will be devoted to Tolstoy’s two novelistic masterpieces, <em>War and Peace </em>and <em>Anna Karenina.</em>  We will also explore some of Tolstoy’s early fiction and one or two of his later works.</p>
<p>Workload: substantial and intense weekly readings; two papers (5-8 pages), a midterm essay and a final examination (focused on close reading of the texts).</p>
<p><strong>Texts include:</strong></p>
<p><em>Childhood</em>; <em>War and Peace</em> (the Pevear and Volokhonsky translation), <em>Anna Karenina </em>(the Pevear and Volokhonsky translation); several short stories.<br />
Leo Tolstoy.  <em>Childhood, Boyhood, and Youth</em>.   Modern Library.  Random House,<br />
0-375-75944-1<br />
Leo Tolstoy.  <em>Anna Karenina</em>.  Penguin, 978-0-14-303500-8<br />
Leo Tolstoy.  <em>Tolstoy’s Short Fiction</em>.  Norton Critical Edition, 978-0393931501<br />
Leo Tolstoy.  <em>War and Peace</em>.  Vintage, 978-1400079988</p>
<p><strong>With concurrent enrollment in Slavic 134R (1 unit), students can write a research paper (10-15 pages) on a topic of individual choice (enrollment in the research unit, upon instructor’s approval, will be open in the Fall).</strong></p>
<p><strong>Prerequisites:</strong>  None. Required readings and lectures in English.  Students with advanced knowledge of Russian are encouraged to do at least some reading in Russian.</p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-2093 -->

			
		
		</div><!-- #content-container -->



	</div><!-- #main -->

<article id="post-10" class="group post-container entry-content">
		<header class="index-header">
		<h1 class="index-title">
			Graduate Program		</h1>
	
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->
	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p>Our PhD graduate program is designed to train future scholars and teachers of Slavic languages and literatures. Students specialize either in literature or linguistics, combining a core curriculum with independent research early in their graduate career.</p>
<p>Students begin with courses leading to a comprehensive Master’s examination, then progress to articulating an individuated program which leads to a research paper, the individually-designed Ph.D. examinations and a dissertation.</p>
<p>Berkeley welcomes students with interdisciplinary interests. Slavic students may pursue official Designated Emphases in Film, Folklore, Women Gender and Sexuality Studies, Critical Theory, Renaissance and Early Modern Studies, etc., as well as individually designed areas of specialization. The Slavic Department works in collaboration with the departments of Comparative Literature, Linguistics, Anthropology, History, Theater, Music, Art History, and with the Institute of Slavic, East European and Eurasian Studies, which houses the Berkeley Program in Soviet and Post-Soviet Studies,  and The Caucasus and Central Asia Program.</p>
<p>Please explore all of the program information using the links to the right.</p>
<p>For more information on professional development, research, and teaching, visit the Graduate Division&#8217;s <a href="http://grad.berkeley.edu/professional-development/guide/">Professional Development Guide and calendar</a>.</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-10 -->

<article id="post-505" class="group post-container entry-content">
		<header class="index-header">
		<h1 class="index-title">
			Dissertations Written in the Department		</h1>
	
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->
	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<h3 style="text-align: left">From 1990 to the present</h3>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>Baerman, Matthew. Free to Fixed Stress in Slavic. 1999.</p>
<p>Barskova, Polina Yuryevna. Enchanted by the Spectacle of Death: Forms of the End in Leningrad Culture (1917-1934). 2006.</p>
<p>Bermel, Neil Halford Andrew. Context and the Lexicon in the Development of Russian Aspect. 1994.</p>
<p>Bernshtein, Evgenii V. Western Models of Sexuality in Russian Modernism. 1998.</p>
<p>Brown, Avram Seth. Modernist Apocrypha: Contexts of the Gospel Plot in Russian Modernism. 1998.</p>
<p>Butler, Francis. Images of Missionaries and Innovative Rulers in East Slavic Literature from Early Times through the reign of Peter the Great. 1991.</p>
<p>Christopher Caes, Historical Contingency and Conceptions of the Self in Stalinist-era and Post-Stalinist Polish Literature and Film, 1950-1960. 2004.</p>
<p>Carlson, Paul Eric. Some Functions of Poetic Diction in A. K. Tolstoy’s “Smert’ Ioanna Groznogo”. 1992.</p>
<p>Comer, William John. The Russian Religious Dissenters and the Literary Culture of the Symbolist Generation. 1992.</p>
<p>Daly, Conor Anthony. Linguistic Change and Cultural Paradigms: the Development of Russian Scholarly Prose (18th-20th Centuries). 1991.</p>
<p>DiCeglie, Margherita M. Pia. The Fate of Humanism in Fin-de-siecle Russia. Vicheslav Ivanov’s Philosophical Speculations and Poetic Vision. The Dialogue of Poetry and Philosophy. 1999.</p>
<p>Elliott, Shawn Kate. The Aesthetics of Russian Folk Religion and <em>The Brothers Karamazov.</em> 1997. (Comparative Literature Department)</p>
<p>Emerson, Alexis Eugene. Alexandr Blok and the Mother-Figure. 1990.</p>
<p>Erley, Laura Mieka. Reclaiming Native Soil: Cultural Mythologies of Soil in Russiaand Its Eastern Borderlands from the 1840s to the 1930s. 2012.</p>
<p>Frazier, Melissa Anne. Frames of the Imagination: Gogol’s “Arabeski” and the Romantic Question of Genre. 1995.</p>
<p>Goeringer, Keith Eliot. The Syntax and Semantics of Adverbs in Russian: Scope, Orientation, and Word Order. 1998.</p>
<p>Henry, Patrick Gilbert. Metarealism and the Question of Russian Postmodernism. 2006.</p>
<p>Herman, David Benjamin Lyle. Representing Otherness: Urban Poverty in Russian Literature from Karamzin to Nekrasov. 1993.</p>
<p>Horowitz, Brian Jay. M. O. Gershenzon and Intellectual Life of Russia’s Silver Age. 1992.</p>
<p>Hruska, Anne. Infected Families: Belonging and Exclusion in the Works of Leo Tolstoy. 2001.</p>
<p>Hudin, Jennifer Irene. Passive Constructions in Russian. 1990.</p>
<p>Johns, Andreas Rainer Bormann. Baba Iaga, the Ambiguous Mother of the Russian Folktale. 1996.</p>
<p>Jones, Janeen. The Tale of Misery-Evil Fortune: The Transformation of Folklore. 1996.</p>
<p>Kabalin, Margaret Mary. Vjekoslav Babukic: His Role as Linguist During the Illyrian Movement. 1993.</p>
<p>Kayiatos, Anastasia Ioanna. Silence and Alterity in Russia after Stalin, 1955-1975. 2012.</p>
<p>Kleespies, Ingrid. Nomad Nation, Wandering Writer: Writing, Travel, and National Identity in Russian and Polish Literature (From the Late Eighteenth Century to the End of the Nineteenth Century). 2004.</p>
<p>Klioutchkine, Konstantine. Russian Literature and the Press, 1860-1914. 2002.</p>
<p>Kresin, Susan C. Third Person Reference in Russian and Czech. 1994.</p>
<p>Kuharski, Allen James. The Theatre of Witold Gombrowicz. 1991.</p>
<p>Kunichika, Michael Mitsuo. The Penchant for the Primitive: Archaeology, Ethnography, and the Aesthetics of Russian Modernism. 2007.</p>
<p>Langer, Ellen R. Individuality and Grammar: Instrumental Singular Variation in Nineteenth-Century Russian Literary Prose. 2001.</p>
<p>Larsen, Karin. The evolution of the system of long and short adjectives in medieval Slavic and Old Russian. 2002.</p>
<p>Leckey, Celia E. Relative Clauses in Medieval Russian Texts. 1992.</p>
<p>Lindsey, Traci Speed. Bulgarian Verbs of Motion: Slavic Verbs in a Balkan Context. 2011.</p>
<p>Lofstedt, Torsten Martin Gustaf. Russian legends about forest spirits in the context of northern European mythology. 1993.</p>
<p>Marsh-Flores, Ann Marie. Literary Collaboration and the Rise of the Russian Woman Writer: or, How Zinaida Volkonskaia Learned to Write in Tsarist Russia. 2004.</p>
<p>Matthews, David B. Case Variation in Contemporary Standard Russian. 1997.</p>
<p>McAnallen, Julia. The History of Predicative Possession in Slavic: Internal Development vs. Language Contact. 2011.</p>
<p>McDevitt Miller, Ann Elizabeth. The Struggle to Create the New Man: The Literary Criticism and Career of Vladimir Friche. 2000.</p>
<p>Merrill, Jessica Evans. The Role of Folkloristics in the Development of Russian Formalist and Czech Structuralist Literary Theory. 2012.</p>
<p>Montgomery, David Jackson. Ivan Bunin and the Persistence of Memory: Recurrence, Mutability, and Desire in Three Major Texts. 1995.</p>
<p>Naiman, Eric. Sexuality and Utopia: the Debate in the Soviet 20s. 1991.</p>
<p>Nesbet, Anne. The Aesthetics of Violence in Russian and East German Literature. 1992.<br />
(Comparative Literature Department)</p>
<p>Nickell, William Scott. Tolstoy in the Public Domain: His Death as a National Narrative. 1998.</p>
<p>Peabody, Mary Foster. Traditional and Individualistic Aspects of the Petitions of the Archpriest Avvakum. 1991.</p>
<p>Perelmutter, Renee. Referential Negation: syntax/semantics of negative construction and their interaction with narrative structure in Modern Russia. 2008.</p>
<p>Porter, Jillian Elizabeth. Money and Mad Ambition: Economies of Russian Literature 1830-1850. 2011.</p>
<p>Powelstock, David Lee. Poet as Officer and Oracle: Mikhail Lermontov’s Aesthetic Mythology. 1994.</p>
<p>Putney, Christopher Richard. Diabolic Conditionality: Nikolaj Gogol’s Aesthetics of Evil. 1996.</p>
<p>Rees, Mary Elinor. Introductions of New Characters in Russian Memoirs: Discourse Constraints on Word Order. 1994.</p>
<p>Rischin, Ruth Solomon. Semen Iushkevich (1868-1927) : the man and his art. 1993.</p>
<p>Schild, Kathryn Douglas. Between Moscow and Baku: National Literatures at the 1934 Congress of Soviet Writers. 2010.</p>
<p>Shull, Sarah Ellen. The Experience of Space: The Privileged Role of Spatial Prefixation in Czech and Russian. 2000.</p>
<p>Somoff, Victoria. From Authority to Author: Russian Prose on the Eve of the Novel, 1820-1850. 2007.</p>
<p>Stoll, Sabine. The Acquisition of Russian Aspect. 2001.</p>
<p>Stone, Jonathan Craig. Conceptualizing &#8220;Symbolism&#8221;: Institutions, Publications, Readers, and the Russian Propagation of an Idea. 2007.</p>
<p>Tapp, Alyson Louise. From the Elegy to the End of the Novel: Literary Experiences of Emotion. 2011.</p>
<p>Vallee, Lillian Wereda. Bear with a Cross: Primordial Tradition in the Work of Czeslaw Milosz. 2003.</p>
<p>Vanchu, Anthony Joseph. Jurij Olesa’s Artistic Prose and Utopian Mythologies of the 1920s. 1990.</p>
<p>Wesling, Molly Williams. The Russian Representation of Napoleon: A Cultural Mythology. 1998.</p>
<p>Wessling, Robert Diedrich. Semyon Nadson and the Cult of the Tuberculer Poet. 1998.</p>
<p>Wiggins, Kathleen Cameron. The Drama in Disguise: Dramatic Modes of Narration and Textual Structure in the Mid-Nineteenth-Century Russian Novel. 2011.</p>
<p>Wolfson,Boris. Staging the Soviet Self: Literature, Theater, and Stalinist Culture, 1929-1939. 2004.</p>

<article id="post-435" class="group post-container entry-content">
		<header class="index-header">
		<h1 class="index-title">
			GRADUATE ADMISSION &#038; HOW TO APPLY		</h1>
	
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->
	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p style="text-align: center;"><strong>The deadline to apply is <u>8:59 PM (Pacific Standard Time) December 15</u>.</strong></p>
<p>The Department accepts applications from students who have or will complete a B.A. before they commence graduate study, and from students who have earned an M.A. from another institution. We expect students who enter with an M.A. in Slavic Languages and Literatures from another institution to have preparation comparable to that provided by the M.A. program at Berkeley. Those with an M.A. from another institution, depending on preparation, may be asked to eliminate discrepancies and/or to take a screening (permission-to-proceed) examination. The format of the screening examination is similar to that of the M.A. examinations. This decision is made by the Graduate Adviser in consultation with other faculty members.</p>
<p>Note that we do not accept applications for a Master’s degree only. It is also important to note that we do not consider applicants who already hold a doctoral degree in any subject, whether obtained at a US institution or abroad.</p>
<p>We select our graduate students on the basis of their record of academic achievement in the past, and their promise of future success in scholarship and teaching. A bachelor&#8217;s degree in Slavic Languages and Literatures or Slavic Studies is preferred but not absolutely required for admission; however, applicants with degrees in other fields must have already undertaken significant coursework in Slavic Studies.</p>
<p>Before you begin your application, be sure you have reviewed the faculty research specialties in the People section of the web site. These are the individuals who will supervise your graduate education and research; it is important to find a good intellectual fit before undertaking any graduate program.</p>
<p>Each section of the graduate application has specific and detailed instructions to help you successfully navigate. In general though, the complete application will include:</p>
<ul>
<li style="list-style-type: none;">
<ul>
<li><strong>Scanned Transcripts </strong>of all schools attended. Official transcripts are required once admitted—never before decisions have been made.</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li style="list-style-type: none;">
<ul>
<li><strong>3 letters of recommendation</strong></li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li style="list-style-type: none;">
<ul>
<li><strong>GRE or TOEFL </strong>exam registration number. The <em>GRE</em> is required of applicants from US schools (and international applicants from schools at which the language of instruction for the Bachelor degree is English&#8211;such as Canada or the UK). We require the <em>TOEFL</em> from all other International applicants; we do not require both, though an international applicant has the <em>option</em> to take the GRE.   As an exception, International applicants may submit scores from IELTS. See the Graduate Division instructions for International applicants for details.<br />
<em>Take your necessary exams well in advance! If we receive exam scores any later than early January, they will be of no use.</em><strong><br />
</strong></li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li style="list-style-type: none;">
<ul>
<li><strong>Statement of Purpose </strong>should be a scholarly piece that demonstrates knowledge, motivation and preparation in your chosen field, academic seriousness and sophistication, and future career goals.</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li style="list-style-type: none;">
<ul>
<li><strong>Personal History </strong>should discuss how/why you have decided upon an academic career. This may include but is not limited to relevant diversity, family background, first generation college education, economic or personal obstacles you have overcome, volunteerism, leadership potential, etc.<br />
<em>The above are distinctly different, may not be combined, and are required by each applicant. There is no set length established for either statement.</em></li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li style="list-style-type: none;">
<ul>
<li><strong>Writing Sample</strong> should be a recent scholarly paper on a topic to do with Slavic literature or linguistics such as a term paper for a course, a senior thesis, or a paper written independently. If you have several short papers which demonstrate different facets of your writing and research abilities, you may submit up to three. We are looking for examples of research ability and some idea of your knowledge in your chosen specialty. There is no set length, though attempting to upload a document with many pictures may exceed the system’s data capacity.</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Applying for Financial Aid is included in the process; be sure to fill out the applicable domestic or International fellowship information sections.</p>
<p>You are not required to complete the whole application in one sitting. Answers are saved and you may return to edit or finish sections until you actually submit the application. We strongly advise that you review everything after a good night’s sleep before submitting the application and paying the fee. Once the application is submitted, you are not allowed to go back and make further edits.</p>
<p>The application system features a status update page for applicants. You may check this page to see if your official exam scores have been received and letters of recommendation submitted.</p>
<p><strong>Timeline: </strong>Applicant files are reviewed for completion, sorted and summarized after the December deadline by the graduate assistant. The faculty admission committee reads and evaluates applications throughout January into early February. Decisions regarding admissions will be announced later in February through mid March. We usually invite prospective admits to visit the department in order to meet the faculty, current students, and their potential co-hort for a few days in early to mid March. The exact timing of this visit is announced in February when students are invited.</p>
<p><strong>Contact information:</strong> For clarification on application procedures, please email the devoted application address &lt;<a href="mailto:slavicadmit@berkeley.edu">slavicadmit@berkeley.edu</a>&gt;.  If you have specific questions about the academic program, please contact the faculty graduate adviser, currently Lyubov Golburt (<a href="mailto:LGolburt@berkeley.edu">LGolburt@berkeley.edu</a>).</p>
<p>Students may also write directly to those with whom they are most interested in working. Prospective students are also encouraged to contact any of our current or former students. Faculty and Students are found on the People page of our website.</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-435 -->

		<div class="book-cover">

				<h2>Books by Alumni</h2>		
						
			<div id="slideshow">
		<div class="slide">
		
		
			<img width="210" height="352" src="http://slavic.berkeley.edu/wp-content/uploads/2015/05/cover_020.jpg" class="attachment-book-cover size-book-cover wp-post-image" alt="" srcset="http://slavic.berkeley.edu/wp-content/uploads/2015/05/cover_020.jpg 500w, http://slavic.berkeley.edu/wp-content/uploads/2015/05/cover_020-179x300.jpg 179w" sizes="(max-width: 210px) 100vw, 210px" />

<article id="post-280" class="group post-container entry-content">
		<header class="index-header">
		<h1 class="index-title">
			LINGUISTIC SPECIALIZATION		</h1>
	
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->
	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p>The Slavic linguistics track of our program has been considerably reduced in recent years. Because students of Slavic linguistics must do most graduate work in individual meetings with faculty, we aim to admit students who already have advanced preparation in the field and who are able and willing to pursue an independent course of professional training. Admitted graduate students will work on an individual course of study designed on the basis of the program described in our Graduate Student Handbook under “Ph.D. Program with Specialization in Linguistics.”</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-280 -->

										</div>

<article id="post-278" class="group post-container entry-content">
		<header class="index-header">
		<h1 class="index-title">
			LITERATURE AND CULTURE		</h1>
	
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->
	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p>The program in literature and culture provides a thorough knowledge of the evolving literary canon along with attendant historical contexts, while encouraging students to acquire expertise in literary and cultural theory. Those considering applying to the MA/PhD program should consult the section on Graduate Admission and How to Apply. Please note that we do not accept applications for a terminal Master’s Degree.</p>
<p>The majority of our students choose Russian as their major language. We encourage students who wish to explore the diverse literary and cultural traditions of Russia, Eastern Europe, and Eurasia. We accept a small number of students who choose Polish, Bulgarian or BCS (Bosnian, Croatian, Serbian) language and literature as their major field. In such cases special programs are worked out and students do much of their graduate work independently. These students normally take Russian as a minor field. Students of Russian literature are highly encouraged to study a second Slavic language.</p>
<p>Please note that we do not administer a Ph.D. Program in Czech, but Czech may be chosen as the second Slavic language.</p>
<p>A detailed description of all coursework and requirements for the MA/PhD program is found in the handbook for continuing students, however a brief description follows:</p>
<h2 style="text-align: center"><strong>The Masters</strong></h2>
<p>The MA stage of the MA/PhD program requires a minimum of 24 letter graded units and is usually completed in 4 semesters. Most M.A. level students in the Slavic program take four courses per semester. The following courses, designed to develop basic professional skills, are offered on a regular basis (as a rule, every other year) and are intended for all students, regardless of specialization.</p>
<p style="padding-left: 30px"><em>SL 201        Advanced Russian Proficiency Maintenance</em><br />
The necessity of this requirement is determined after admission by testing for Russian proficiency.<br />
<em>SL 204        Russian Composition and Style</em><br />
<em>SL 210        Old Church Slavic </em><br />
<em>SL 222        Descriptive Grammar of Russian </em><br />
<em>SL 281        Proseminar: Aims &amp; Methods of Literary Scholarship</em><br />
<em>SL 280        Studies in Slavic Literature and Linguistics (research seminars)</em></p>
<p>The following literature courses, also offered on a regular basis, are elective. In practice, most students choose to take all or most of these courses during the first four years of the MA/PhD program:</p>
<p style="padding-left: 30px"><em>SL 239        Slavic Literary Theory </em><br />
<em>SL 243        The Russian Novel and Literatures of Western Europe </em><br />
<em>SL 245A      Sentimentalism and Romanticism </em><br />
<em>SL 245B      Realism</em><br />
<em>SL 246A      Modernism</em><br />
<em>SL 246B      Contemporary Russian Literature </em><br />
<em>SL 248        Topics in Russian Cultural History</em><br />
<em>SL 287        Poetry, alternately nineteenth or twentieth century</em></p>
<p style="padding-left: 30px"><span style="text-decoration: underline">Slavic Colloquium (SL 200)</span>: Students are required to register (for no unit credit) and attend every semester of the program.</p>
<p style="padding-left: 30px"><span style="text-decoration: underline">Research seminars (SL 280)</span>: M.A. students usually participate in one or more seminars. Topics for seminars vary and are announced a semester in advance. Participation in at least one seminar, resulting in completion of a research paper for a letter grade, is required of M.A. students.</p>
<p>The comprehensive Master’s examinations consist of two 4-hour written examinations followed by a 90 minute oral examination. The written exams are scheduled approximately one week apart and one week before the oral. The examinations are designed to test the student&#8217;s knowledge of the basic material, the ability to develop arguments and discussions, and to assess the capacity for original critical thinking.<br />
Written examinations with a specialization in Russian literature cover:</p>
<p style="padding-left: 60px">Exam I (two essays). 1790s to 1840s and 1840s to 1890s<br />
Exam II (two essays). 1890s to 1920s and 1920s to 1990s</p>
<p>An M.A. candidate may pass and be recommended to continue in the Ph.D. program, pass but not be recommended to continue, fail with a recommendation to attempt a second examination, or fail and leave the program at the end of that semester.</p>
<h2 style="text-align: center"><strong>The Ph.D.</strong></h2>
<p>All students in the Slavic Languages and Literatures Program normally continue taking graduate-level course work for two years beyond conferral of the M.A. as they acquire professional expertise. They continue to take research seminars, language courses, pedagogy and independent studies courses. In addition, Ph.D. students with specialization in Russian literature are expected to broaden their knowledge to include early modern and pre-modern periods (the 18th century and earlier). The following courses are offered on a regular basis (usually, every third year):</p>
<p style="padding-left: 30px"><em>SL 214           Medieval Orthodox Slavic Texts</em> (varied content; may be repeated for credit)<br />
<em>SL 231           History of the Russian Literature Language</em><br />
<em>SL 241A/B     Old Russian Literature, Kievan and Muscovite</em> (varied content; may be repeated)<br />
<em>SL 242           Eighteenth-century Russian Literature</em><br />
<em>SL 248           Topics in Russian Cultural History</em> (on a pre-modern topic)<br />
<em>SL 280           Studies in Slavic Literature and Linguistics</em> (on a pre-modern topic)<br />
<em>SL 285           Eastern Christianity: History and Thought</em></p>
<p style="padding-left: 30px"><span style="text-decoration: underline">Requirements in pre-modern literature</span>:<br />
SL 242,<em> Eighteenth-century Russian Literature</em> and any one other course, including appropriate SL 280 research seminars. (Prerequisite: SL 210, <em>Old Church Slavic</em>, is taken as a part of the M.A. program.).</p>
<p><strong>Major and Minor Fields:</strong> Ph.D. students who specialize in Russian literature are expected to acquire competence in two fields: a major and a minor field. Students who select Russian literature as the major field are encouraged to study another Slavic language and literature as a minor field of study. As an alternative, other minor fields may be selected and designed, for example, Russian/East European film, theater, history, art, etc. Individually designed minor fields will include course work on historical and methodological issues of the discipline, taken in an appropriate department. (Thus, a minor in Russian art would include course work in the History of Art Department; a minor in Russian history would entail course work in the Department of History.) In this way, Berkeley students receive professional preparation both in literature and in broader areas of culture. All minor fields must be approved by the Graduate Adviser.</p>
<p>Those who pursue a Ph.D. in Polish, Bulgarian or BCS (Bosnian, Croatian, Serbian) language and literature are strongly encouraged to choose a minor in Russian language and literature; such students follow an individual program approved by the Graduate Adviser.</p>
<p><strong>Research Requirement (&#8220;qualifying paper&#8221; or &#8220;publishable paper&#8221;):</strong> All graduate students are required to submit an extended research paper (on a topic of their own choice) to satisfy the Departmental research requirement. The final version of the publishable paper must be approved by the end of the 3rd year.</p>
<p><strong>The Ph.D. Qualifying Examinations</strong> consist of two written examinations, four hours each, and one three-hour oral examination. The Ph.D. examinations, test the candidate&#8217;s ability to analyze literary texts and to conceptualize the place and function of major texts (as well as events in literary history) within a broad cultural context that includes literary evolution and the development of genres, the history of Russian and European culture, social institutions of literature, the interrelation of Russian literature with other world literatures, etc. The candidate is also expected to demonstrate knowledge of and skill in applying theoretical concepts dealing with language, style, genre, narrative technique, versification, etc. Upon successful completion of the Qualifying Exam, the student applies to the Graduate Division for Advancement to Ph.D. candidacy.</p>
<p><strong>The Dissertation Prospectus</strong> must be completed and approved by the end of the semester following the QE. The prospectus articulates a student’s dissertation topic and includes a working bibliography. At this point formal course attendance ends and students begin registering in the required units under SL 299 usually supervised by the intended chair of their dissertation committee.</p>
<p><strong>The Doctoral Dissertation</strong> represents the final demonstration, in the graduate program, of a student&#8217;s research and scholarly abilities, and constitutes an original contribution to the field of study. The dissertation must receive the unanimous approval of the committee members in order for it to be accepted as final completion of the degree requirements.</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-278 -->

		<div class="book-cover">

				<h2>Books by Alumni</h2>		
						
			<div id="slideshow">
		<div class="slide">
		
		
			<img width="210" height="296" src="http://slavic.berkeley.edu/wp-content/uploads/2015/05/cover_014.jpg" class="attachment-book-cover size-book-cover wp-post-image" alt="" srcset="http://slavic.berkeley.edu/wp-content/uploads/2015/05/cover_014.jpg 500w, http://slavic.berkeley.edu/wp-content/uploads/2015/05/cover_014-213x300.jpg 213w" sizes="(max-width: 210px) 100vw, 210px" />
		
		</div>
		
		
		<div class="slide">
		
		
			<img width="210" height="319" src="http://slavic.berkeley.edu/wp-content/uploads/2015/05/cover_031.jpg" class="attachment-book-cover size-book-cover wp-post-image" alt="" srcset="http://slavic.berkeley.edu/wp-content/uploads/2015/05/cover_031.jpg 500w, http://slavic.berkeley.edu/wp-content/uploads/2015/05/cover_031-197x300.jpg 197w" sizes="(max-width: 210px) 100vw, 210px" />

<article id="post-282" class="group post-container entry-content">
		<header class="index-header">
		<h1 class="index-title">
			SUPPORT &#038; TEACHING		</h1>
	
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->
	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p>The department strives to support all our graduate students for six years, and, where necessary, for an additional year. Support comes from a variety of sources: competitive university fellowships, departmental funds, as well as teaching and research appointments. As a rule we do not admit students whom we cannot support.</p>
<p>The Slavic Department provides the majority of its students the opportunity to teach as Graduate Student Instructors, or “GSIs,” for which they receive ongoing training in pedagogy. Appointments are made in the spring semester prior to the upcoming academic year. Graduate students teach Russian and other Slavic language as well as individually-designed courses in literature through the university’s Reading and Composition program. Slavic students have also taught in the departments of Comparative Literature, Linguistics, and Women’s Studies as well as in the Film Studies programs.</p>
<p>It is rare that we need to hire a student from another department to teach one of our language or Reading and Composition courses. If you are a student from outside the department interested in a teaching position with us, please first contact the Department Chair to inquire if the department is in need of additional GSI’s.</p>
<p>Students should be aware of the information and resources regarding teaching that are available both in the Slavic library as well as online:</p>
<p><a href="http://gsi.berkeley.edu">http://gsi.berkeley.edu</a><br />
<a href="https://gsi.berkeley.edu/teachingguide/readingcomp/index.html">https://gsi.berkeley.edu/teachingguide/readingcomp/index.html</a><br />
<a href="https://gsi.berkeley.edu/teachingguide/readingcomp/resources.html">https://gsi.berkeley.edu/teachingguide/readingcomp/resources.html</a></p>
<h1>ANNUAL REVIEWS OF PROGRESS AND FUNDING</h1>
<p>Continuing students are given an annual review, usually early in the Spring semesters, to plan funding for research, language acquisition and the coming academic year’s teaching.</p>
<p>In general, evaluation of student progress and performance is a continuous process which takes place throughout the year, especially during each semester’s TeleBEAR registration advising period with the Graduate Adviser. He or she also schedules interviews with students at the end of the first year and following major examinations. Those students who are having academic difficulties or whose candidacy for a degree is in jeopardy are given special attention.</p>
<p>Each spring semester—due in February—all students (those advanced and not advanced to candidacy) are reviewed by the Department Admissions and Funding Committee. The Continued Funding Application and Progress Report is required of all students (including first year). This form has three purposes: it helps the Department plan teaching assignments and budget financial support for the coming summer and academic year; it provides an opportunity for students to assess their academic progress; it allows the committee to review progress and achievements.<br />
Students with serious deficiencies in their performance or rate of progress will be informed in writing of the specific nature of the deficiency, what steps need to be taken to correct the problem, and given a timetable according to which the student is expected to make improvement. Students with serious deficiencies may be placed on probationary status in the Department, but this is rare.</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-282 -->

<article id="post-284" class="group post-container entry-content">
		<header class="index-header">
		<h1 class="index-title">
			TRAVEL		</h1>
	
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->
	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p>Our students frequently travel to Russia, Eastern and Central Europe, the Caucasus and Central Asia, to pursue language study or independent research over the summer. Graduate students additionally participate in professional conferences within and beyond the United States. The annual <em>Continued Funding Application and Progress Report</em> is the medium through which students request additional funds. While the Department makes every effort to support students’ travel and research needs, students are encouraged to first consult the following websites for funding purposes:</p>
<p>Graduate Division: <a href="http://grad.berkeley.edu/financial/fellowships/">http://grad.berkeley.edu/financial/fellowships/</a><br />
Graduate Assembly: <a href="https://ga.berkeley.edu/funding/travel-grants">https://ga.berkeley.edu/funding/travel-grants</a><br />
ISEEES: <a href="http://iseees.berkeley.edu/iseees_fellowships">http://iseees.berkeley.edu/iseees_fellowships</a> ; <a href="http://iseees.berkeley.edu/FLAS">http://iseees.berkeley.edu/FLAS</a></p>
<p>Students may also apply for Fulbright, IREX or ACLS grants in order to pursue longer research projects during the academic year.</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-284 -->

										</div>
			</div><!-- #content -->

<article id="post-651" class="group post-container entry-content">
		<header class="index-header">
		<h1 class="index-title">
			Department Forms		</h1>
	
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->
	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p><em>The following forms are for current members of the Slavic department.  (For continuing graduate student forms, click <a title="FORMS FOR CONTINUING STUDENTS" href="http://slavic.berkeley.edu/graduate/forms-for-continuing-students/">here</a>.)</em></p>
<p><strong>REIMBURSEMENT FORMS:</strong></p>
<p>Please complete the form, print and sign a paper copy, and attach all relevant receipts. Return completed forms and documents to Elizabeth LaVarge-Baptista in 6311 Dwinelle or to her mailbox in 6303 Dwinelle.</p>
<p><em>Please do not save copies of these forms for future use &#8212; they are frequently updated, and you will find the latest versions here.</em></p>
<p><a href="https://csstravelform.berkeley.edu/">Domestic Travel</a></p>
<p><em>Note: This form needs to be completed online in order to utilize the drop down menus.</em></p>
<p><a href="http://live-slavic.pantheon.berkeley.edu/wp-content/uploads/2014/06/Intl-Travel-Form-7-17-2015.pdf">International Travel</a></p>
<p><em>Note: For this form to print with proper formatting, you should download to Adobe first, then print.</em></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="https://aoprals.state.gov/web920/per_diem.asp">Foreign Per Diem Rates</a>, USD, based on the month in which the traveler arrived in the country.</li>
</ul>
<p><a href="http://live-slavic.pantheon.berkeley.edu/wp-content/uploads/2014/06/Entertainment-Reimbursement-Form-6-16-2016.pdf">Entertainment</a></p>
<p><em>Note: For this form to print with proper formatting, you should download to Adobe first, then print.</em></p>
<p><a href="http://live-slavic.pantheon.berkeley.edu/wp-content/uploads/2014/06/General-Reimbursement-Request-Form1.pdf">General Reimbursements</a></p>
<p><a href="http://live-slavic.pantheon.berkeley.edu/wp-content/uploads/2014/06/Certificate-of-Lost-or-Missing-Receipt.pdf">Certificate of Lost or Missing Receipt</a> Travel and Entertainment: for expenses $75+ for which there is no itemized receipt or no receipt at all. General: required if missing an itemized receipt or proof of payment.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>For examples, see below:</p>
<p><a href="http://live-slavic.pantheon.berkeley.edu/wp-content/uploads/2014/06/domestic-travel_Page_1.jpg">Domestic Travel Reimbursement INSTRUCTIONS</a> <a href="http://live-slavic.pantheon.berkeley.edu/wp-content/uploads/2014/06/domestic-travel_Page_2.jpg">(pg 2)</a></p>
<p><a href="http://live-slavic.pantheon.berkeley.edu/wp-content/uploads/2014/06/International-Form.jpg">International Travel Reimbursement INSTRUCTIONS</a></p>
<p><a href="http://live-slavic.pantheon.berkeley.edu/wp-content/uploads/2014/06/Entertainment-Reimbursement-TO-ANNOTATE.jpg">Entertainment Reimbursement INSTRUCTIONS</a></p>
<p><a href="http://live-slavic.pantheon.berkeley.edu/wp-content/uploads/2014/06/General-Reimbursement.jpg">General Reimbursement INSTRUCTIONS</a></p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

<article id="post-9" class="group post-container entry-content">
		<header class="index-header">
		<h1 class="index-title">
			Undergraduate Program		</h1>
	
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->
	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<h3></h3>
<h3>Attention Slavic majors and minors: new courses open for Fall 2017</h3>
<p><a href="http://slavic.berkeley.edu/courses/advanced-readings-in-russian-east-european-and-eurasian-languages/">Slavic 100L: Advanced Readings in Russian, East European, and Eurasian Languages</a></p>
<p><a href="http://slavic.berkeley.edu/courses/research-in-russian-east-european-and-eurasian-studies/">Slavic 100R: Research in Russian, East European, and Eurasian Studies</a></p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<hr />
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>The Department offers several different major and minor tracks (see <a href="http://slavic.berkeley.edu/undergraduate/major-tracks/">MAJOR TRACKS</a> and <a href="http://live-slavic.pantheon.berkeley.edu/undergraduate/minor-tracks/">MINOR TRACKS</a>). With the choice of tracks, students may develop a program of study that suits their needs and interests. There is flexibility in the number of languages, literatures and cultures studied and the types of courses taken. Whereas some students focus primarily on literature and language, others pursue a program that embraces a variety of related disciplines. All our majors have the opportunity to work closely with faculty in their areas of interest. Many of our students pursue a double major, with the other major in History, Political Science, Economics, Business, Peace and Conflict Studies, and other disciplines. Although much of what we teach is specific to the Slavic field, the faculty is dedicated to helping students develop skills in English expository writing, in interpreting texts, in oral communication, in research, and in critical thinking. As students in a small department, our majors benefit from the accessibility of their teachers and the community of fellow students.</p>
<p>For non-majors, we offer a number of courses (taught in English) that fulfill breadth requirements in Arts and Literature, Philosophy and Values, Historical Studies, Social and Behavioral Sciences, and International Studies, as well as courses in the University program of Freshmen and Sophomore seminars. See <a href="http://slavic.berkeley.edu/courses-2/">COURSES</a>. Learn more about the Slavic Department&#8217;s <a href="http://slavic.berkeley.edu/wp-content/uploads/2014/04/SlavicStudyAbroad1.pdf">Study Abroad options here</a>.</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-9 -->

										</div>
			</div><!-- #content -->



	</div><!-- #main -->

<article id="post-301" class="group post-container entry-content">
		<header class="index-header">
		<h1 class="index-title">
			Declaring a Major		</h1>
	
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->
	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p>All students must see the Major Advisor for consultation and study list planning and approval prior to pursuing a major track.</p>
<p>Students may declare their major upon entry to the university. However, we encourage one year of study in the student’s major language prior to declaration.</p>
<p>Consult each track requirements for details.</p>
<h2>HONORS PROGRAM</h2>
<p>Slavic majors with a minimum GPA of 3.3 overall and in courses for the major are invited to consult with members of the faculty and the Major Advisor in the spring of their junior year about the honors program and a thesis topic. Requirements for the honors program in Slavic include an honors thesis course (H195) on a topic of individual interest. The student writes a thesis under the direction of a member of the faculty (as thesis director) and one additional faculty member (who also reads the completed thesis). The work on the thesis often begins in Slavic 100 in the Fall and is then continued in H195 in the Spring semester.</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-301 -->

										</div>
			</div><!-- #content -->

<article id="post-305" class="group post-container entry-content">
		<header class="index-header">
		<h1 class="index-title">
			Major/Minor Requirements for Heritage Speakers		</h1>
	
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->
	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p>Heritage speakers include those who grew up in Russian speaking families or communities, but without a standard Russian language educational background. Heritage speakers may select any major or minor track offered by the Department <em>except</em> the minor in Russian language. The requirements may change depending on each student&#8217;s language proficiency and academic interests. Consult the Major Advisor for language testing and individualized plan of study.</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-305 -->

										</div>
			</div><!-- #content -->

<article id="post-307" class="group post-container entry-content">
		<header class="index-header">
		<h1 class="index-title">
			Language Instruction		</h1>
	
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->
	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p>We regularly offer instruction in Russian, Polish, Czech, and BCS (Bosnian/Croatian/Serbian), as well as the non-Slavic languages Armenian and Hungarian. The department offers special classes for heritage speakers of Russian and courses in Russian/English, English/Russian oral and written translation.</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-307 -->

										</div>
			</div><!-- #content -->

<article id="post-294" class="group post-container entry-content">
		<header class="index-header">
		<h1 class="index-title">
			Major Tracks		</h1>
	
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->
	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p>The department offers two different major tracks. The major track in Russian/East European/Eurasian Languages and Cultures offers an interdisciplinary “area studies” approach. For this major track, divided into <a href="#concentrationinrussian">Russian</a> or <a href="#eaeurlang">East European/Eurasian</a> concentrations, two years of language study (or the equivalent) are required. The major track in <a href="#russianlanglit">Russian Language and Literature</a> focuses specifically on Russian language and literature. It requires three years of language coursework (or the equivalent).</p>
<p>An overall grade-point average of 2.0 in upper-division courses applied to the major program is required. All courses fulfilling major requirements must be taken for a letter grade.</p>
<p>The Slavic Department’s majors are flexible: we take into account individual students’ academic interests, prior exposure to the area, and knowledge of its languages. We welcome double majors.</p>
<p>These major tracks are updated as of November 2017.</p>
<h2>Major Track in Russian/East European/ Eurasian Languages and Cultures</h2>
<p>This major track integrates the study of languages and cultures of a large area: Russia, East Central Europe, Southeastern Europe, and Eurasia. Students design their own programs by selecting courses offered by the Slavic and other departments and programs, such as Anthropology, Economics, Geography, Global Studies, History, Journalism, Legal Studies, Political Science, Sociology, Theater, Dance, and Performance Studies. While all majors in this track will gain knowledge of the whole area, the program also allows each student to concentrate on a particular language and culture. Students are advised to see the Undergraduate Advisor in advance to prepare an individualized study plan.</p>
<p>Languages regularly offered by our department that can be used for this track are: Russian, Armenian, BCS (Bosnian/Croatian/Serbian), Czech, Hungarian and Polish.</p>
<p>Students may declare the major after the completion of two semesters of a language (or equivalent) and one lower division course, Slavic 50.</p>
<p>The Department highly recommends additional exposure to language through coursework, intensive summer language programs, or the Education Abroad Program.</p>
<h3><strong><a id="concentrationinrussian"></a>Concentration in Russian:<br />
</strong></h3>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><strong><em>Lower-division (23 units):</em></strong></p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Four semesters of Russian (Russian 1, 2, 3, 4)</strong> or the equivalent as determined by examination.<br />
Students with prior knowledge of the languages should consult the Major Advisor for language testing and placement. 20 units.</li>
<li><strong>Slavic 50:</strong> <em>Introduction to Russian/East European/Eurasian Cultures</em>. (With permission of the Major Advisor, it may be possible to substitute another lower-division course, e.g. Slavic 39, 45, or 46). 3 units.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong><em>Upper-division (25-31 units):</em></strong></p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Slavic 100</strong> (Seminar: Russian, East European, and Eurasian Cultures). 3 units.</li>
<li><strong>Six courses chosen from the upper-division courses offered by the Slavic Department</strong>, or area-relevant courses from other departments and programs, such as Anthropology, Economics, Geography, Global Studies, History, Journalism, Legal Studies, Political Science, Sociology, Theater, Dance and Performance Studies. 18-24 units.
<ul>
<li>Select from <b>Slavic 130, 140, 150, 160, 170 or Armenian 120 series</b></li>
<li>Up to two of these six courses can be replaced by <a href="http://slavic.berkeley.edu/courses/advanced-readings-in-russian-east-european-and-eurasian-languages/"><strong>Slavic 100L </strong></a>(readings in the original language) or <a href="http://slavic.berkeley.edu/courses/research-in-russian-east-european-and-eurasian-studies/"><strong>Slavic 100R</strong></a> (research). 1 unit each.</li>
<li>With permission of the Major Advisor, up to two upper-division language courses may be counted among electives.</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<h3><strong><a id="eaeurlang"></a>Concentration in Armenian, Bosnian/Croatian/Serbian, Czech, Hungarian, or Polish:<br />
</strong></h3>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><strong><em>Lower-division (11 units):</em></strong></p>
<ul>
<li><strong>2 semesters of introductory language</strong> or equivalent, as determined by examination. 8 units.<br />
<strong>Armenian 1A-B</strong><br />
<strong> BCS 27A-B</strong><br />
<strong> Czech 26A-B</strong><br />
<strong> Hungarian 1A-B</strong><br />
<strong> Polish 25A-B</strong><br />
Students with prior knowledge of the language should see the Undergraduate Advisor for language testing and placement.</li>
<li><strong>Slavic 50:</strong> <em>Introduction to Russian/East European/Eurasian Cultures</em>. (With permission of the Major Advisor, it may be possible to substitute another lower-division course, e.g. Slavic 39, 45, or 46). 3 units.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong><em>Upper-division (25-31 units):</em></strong></p>
<ul>
<li><strong>2 semesters of continuing language</strong> or equivalent. 8 units<br />
<strong>Armenian 101A-B</strong><br />
<strong> BCS 117A-B</strong><br />
<strong> Czech 116A-B</strong><br />
<strong> Hungarian 100</strong><br />
<strong> Polish 115A-B<br />
</strong>Students with prior knowledge of the language should see the Undergraduate Advisor for language testing and placement.</li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li style="list-style-type: none;"></li>
<li><b>Slavic 100 </b>(Seminar: Russian, East European, and Eurasian Cultures). 3 units</li>
<li><strong>Five courses chosen from the upper-division courses offered by the Slavic Department</strong> or area-relevant courses from other departments and programs, such as Anthropology, Economics, Geography, Global Studies, History, Journalism, Legal Studies, Political Science, Sociology, Theater, Dance and Performance Studies. 14-20 units
<ul>
<li>Select from <b>Slavic 130, 140, 150, 160, 170 or Armenian 120 series </b></li>
<li>Up to two of these five courses can be replaced by  <a href="http://slavic.berkeley.edu/courses/advanced-readings-in-russian-east-european-and-eurasian-languages/"><strong>Slavic 100L </strong></a>(readings in the original language) or <a href="http://slavic.berkeley.edu/courses/research-in-russian-east-european-and-eurasian-studies/"><strong>Slavic 100R</strong></a> (research). 1 unit each.</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<h2><a id="russianlanglit"></a>Major Track in Russian Language and Literature</h2>
<p>This major track focuses on the study of Russian language, literature, and culture. Students are advised to see the Undergraduate Advisor in advance to prepare an individualized study plan.</p>
<p>Students may declare the major after the completion of two semesters of Russian (or equivalent) and either Slavic 45 or 46.</p>
<h3><strong>REQUIREMENTS:</strong></h3>
<p><strong><em>Lower-division (28 units):</em></strong></p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Four semesters of Russian (Russian 1, 2, 3, 4)</strong> or the equivalent as determined by examination. Students with prior knowledge of the languages should consult the Major Advisor for language testing and placement. 20 units.</li>
<li>A two-semester survey of nineteenth- and twentieth-century Russian literature. 8 units.<br />
<strong>Slavic 45</strong> (Nineteenth-Century Russian Literature)<br />
<strong>Slavic</strong> <strong>46</strong> (Twentieth-Century Russian Literature)</li>
</ul>
<p><strong><em>Upper-division (25-31 units):</em></strong></p>
<ul>
<li><b>Slavic 100 </b>(Seminar: Russian, East European, and Eurasian Cultures). 3 units</li>
<li>Two semesters of Advanced Russian language. 8 units.<br />
<strong>Russian 103A</strong><br />
<strong>Russian 103B<br />
</strong>Students with prior knowledge of Russian may substitute specialized courses, such as Russian 102 (Readings in Specialized Russian), Russian 105 (Advanced Translation), Russian 120 (Advanced Russian Conversation and Communication).</li>
<li>One course on literature and culture with readings in Russian. 4 units<br />
<b>Slavic 180, 181, 182, 188, or 190</b></li>
<li>Two courses on Russian literature and culture in English translation. 8 units<br />
<b>Slavic 130-140 series.</b></li>
<li><strong>Two courses chosen from the upper-division courses in Russian literature and culture offered by the Slavic Department </strong>or area-relevant courses from other departments and programs, such as Anthropology, Economics, Geography, Global Studies, History, Journalism, Legal Studies, Political Science, Sociology, Theater, Dance and Performance Studies. 8 units<br />
<b>Slavic 130-140 series</b></p>
<ul>
<li>Up to 2 courses can be replaced by  <a href="http://slavic.berkeley.edu/courses/advanced-readings-in-russian-east-european-and-eurasian-languages/"><strong>Slavic 100L </strong></a>(readings in the original language) or <a href="http://slavic.berkeley.edu/courses/research-in-russian-east-european-and-eurasian-studies/"><strong>Slavic 100R</strong></a> (research). 1 unit each.</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

<article id="post-299" class="group post-container entry-content">
		<header class="index-header">
		<h1 class="index-title">
			Minor Tracks		</h1>
	
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->
	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p>The Department offers minors in: (a) <a href="#armenian">Armenian Studies</a>, (b) <a href="#ruscult">Russian culture</a>, (c) <a href="#ruslanguage">Russian language</a>, (d) <a href="#rusliterature">Russian literature</a>, (e) <a href="#eeealangcult">East European/Eurasian Languages and/or Cultures</a>.</p>
<p><strong>Requirements:</strong> The minor is in a field academically distinct from the student’s major. An overall grade-point average of 2.0 in upper-division courses applied to the minor program is required. At least three of the required upper division courses must be taken at Berkeley.</p>
<p>All courses fulfilling minor requirements must be taken for a letter grade.</p>
<p>Updated as of November 2017.<br />
<a id="armenian"></a></p>
<h2>Minor in Armenian Studies</h2>
<p>The minor in Armenian Studies provides students with a broad knowledge of Armenian language, history, literature, and culture. The minor is academically distinct from the student’s major. The program is offered by the Department of Slavic Languages and Literatures in cooperation with the Armenian Studies Program administered by the Institute of Slavic, East European, and Eurasian Studies (<a href="https://iseees.berkeley.edu/">ISEEES</a>).</p>
<p><strong>Requirements: </strong></p>
<p>An overall grade-point average of 2.0 in upper-division courses applied to the minor program is required. At least three of the required upper-division courses must be taken at UC Berkeley. All courses fulfilling minor requirements must be taken for a letter grade. Students may declare the minor after the completion of two semesters of Armenian language (or equivalent).</p>
<p><strong>Prerequisite:</strong></p>
<p>Two semesters of Introductory Armenian (<strong>Armenian 1A and 1B, </strong>3 units each) or equivalent as determined by examination.<br />
Students with prior knowledge of the language should consult the Major Advisor for examination and language placement.</p>
<p><strong>Upper-division requirements: </strong>5 courses, 18 units.</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Armenian 101A and 101B</strong>: Continuing Armenian. 3 units each. or equivalent as determined by examination</li>
<li><strong>Three upper-division courses </strong>to be chosen from the University’s offerings in Armenian literature, culture, and history, including:
<ul>
<li><strong>Armenian 124: </strong>Armenian Literature in a Social Context. 4 units</li>
<li><strong>Armenian 126: </strong>Armenian Culture and Film, 4 units</li>
<li><strong>History 177A: </strong>Armenia from Ethnogenesis to the Dark Ages, 4 units</li>
<li><strong>History 177B: </strong>Armenia: From Pre-modern Empires to the Present, 4 units</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Other area-relevant courses can be used to satisfy the three upper- division courses requirement by permission of the Slavic Department’s Undergraduate Adviser.</p>
<p><a id="ruscult"></p>
<h2>Minor in Russian Culture</h2>
<p></a><br />
<strong>Prerequisite:</strong></p>
<p>Four semesters of elementary/intermediate Russian (<strong>Russian 1, 2, 3, &amp; 4</strong> or equivalent). Russian heritage speakers should see language placement approval instructions.</p>
<p><strong>Upper-division requirements: </strong>5 courses, 15-20 units:</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Five upper-division courses</strong> (3 or 4 units each) in Russian language and/or Russian and other Slavic literatures and cultures.
<ul>
<li>These courses may be chosen in any combination by the student, in consultation with the Major Advisor.</li>
<li>A course from another related program (for example, Comparative Literature) may be substituted with approval of the Major Advisor.</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<h2>Minor in Russian Language</h2>
<p><strong>Prerequisite:</strong></p>
<p>Four semesters of elementary/intermediate Russian (<strong>Russian 1, 2, 3, &amp; 4</strong> or equivalent). Students with prior knowledge of the language should consult the Major Adviser for language placement.</p>
<p><strong>Upper-division requirements: </strong>5 courses, 17-20 units</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Russian 103A and B</strong>: Advanced Russian. 4 units each.</li>
<li><strong>Three courses with advanced readings in Russian,</strong> to be chosen from:
<ul>
<li><strong>Russian 102: </strong>Readings in Specialized Russian. 3 units</li>
<li><strong>Russian 105: </strong>Advanced Russian/English/Russian Translation. 3 units</li>
<li><strong>Slavic 180: </strong>Studies in Russian Literature. 4 units</li>
<li><strong>Slavic 181: </strong>Readings in Russian Literature. 4 units</li>
<li><strong>Slavic 182: </strong>Pushkin. 4 units</li>
<li><strong>Slavic 188: </strong>Russian Prose. 4 units</li>
<li><strong>Slavic 190: </strong>Russian Culture Taught in Russian: Country, Identity, and Language. 4 units</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<h2>Minor in Russian Literature</h2>
<p><strong>Prerequisite:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Slavic 45: </strong>Nineteenth-Century Russian Literature. 4 units</li>
</ul>
<p style="text-align: left;"><em>or</em></p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Slavic 46: </strong>Twentieth-Century Russian Literature. 4 units</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Upper-division Requirements: </strong>5 courses, 15-20 units.</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Five upper-division courses </strong>on the literature or culture of Russia, selected from the Department offerings.</li>
</ul>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<h2>Minor in East European/Eurasian Languages and/or Cultures with Concentrations in Armenian, Bosnian/Croatian/Serbian, Czech, Hungarian, or Polish</h2>
<p><strong>Prerequisites:</strong></p>
<p>Two semesters of language at the introductory level, or equivalent as determined by examination. 8 units</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Armenian 1A-B</strong></li>
<li><strong>Bosnian/Croatian/Serbian 27A-B</strong></li>
<li><strong>Czech 26A-B</strong></li>
<li><strong>Hungarian 1A-B</strong></li>
<li><strong>Polish 25A-B</strong></li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Upper-Division Requirements: </strong>5 courses, 17-20 units.<strong><br />
</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Two semesters of language at the continuing level.* 8 units.
<ul>
<li><strong>Armenian 101A-B</strong></li>
<li><strong>Bosnian/Croatian/Serbian 117A-B</strong></li>
<li><strong>Czech 116A-B</strong></li>
<li><strong>Hungarian 100</strong></li>
<li><strong>Polish 115A-B</strong></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><strong>Three courses in the relevant literatures or cultures</strong>, or substitutes approved by the major advisor. 9-12 units.</li>
<li>*If upper-division language requirements are passed by examination: <strong>5 courses in the relevant literatures or cultures </strong>chosen from the Slavic department offerings and area-relevant courses from other departments and programs, such as Anthropology, Economics, Geography, Global Studies, History, Journalism, Legal Studies, Political Science, Sociology, Theater, Dance, and Performance Studies. 15-20 units</li>
</ul>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-299 -->

<article id="post-309" class="group post-container entry-content">
		<header class="index-header">
		<h1 class="index-title">
			Outside the Classroom		</h1>
	
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->
	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p>Our campus hosts many Slavic-related lectures, concerts, films, conferences, and other events. A weekly Russian conversation hour is one of the Berkeley Slavic Department&#8217;s most lively institutions. The Polish Circle and Czech Circle meet regularly for discussions and social events, and a BCS cultural group is being organized this year. Film showings, of classic and contemporary films from Russia and other countries, are periodically organized by graduate students.</p>
<h2>Education Abroad</h2>
<p>The Slavic Department actively encourages students to participate in study abroad programs in Russia and other East European countries. Through the University of California’s Study Abroad Program, students may spend the fall or spring semester in St. Petersburg and do intensive work on Russian language, literature, and culture. UC-sponsored programs emphasize Russian language as well as cultural studies. For information see the <a href="http://live-slavic.pantheon.berkeley.edu/wp-content/uploads/2014/04/SlavicStudyAbroad1.pdf">Slavic Department&#8217;s information poster</a>. Other institutions also offer programs in Russia and East European countries, both during the school year and summer. Please consult with the Major Advisor for information about these programs. Read more about studying abroad on the <a href="http://eap.ucop.edu/Pages/default.aspx">Berkeley Programs for Study Abroad website.</a></p>
<h2>After Graduation</h2>
<p>In the world today, in-depth understanding of another culture and knowledge of another language are increasingly valued. Graduates from our department are finding that the recent political, social, and economic changes in the former Soviet Union, East Central Europe, and Southeastern Europe have resulted in an increasing number of opportunities for employment, both in this country and abroad, in fields such as education, journalism, business, diplomacy, social work, health, and environmental affairs. A Slavic major also provides excellent preparation for graduate studies in a number of areas, as well as a sound background in the liberal arts for those wishing to attend professional schools (including law school).</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-309 -->

<article id="post-1344" class="group post-container entry-content">
		<header class="index-header">
		<h1 class="index-title">
			Study Abroad		</h1>
	
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->
	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p>The Slavic Department actively encourages students to participate in study abroad programs in Russia and other East European countries. Through the University of California’s Study Abroad Program, students may spend the fall or spring semester in St. Petersburg and do intensive work on Russian language, literature, and culture. UC-sponsored programs emphasize Russian language as well as cultural studies. For information see the <a href="http://live-slavic.pantheon.berkeley.edu/wp-content/uploads/2014/04/SlavicStudyAbroad1.pdf">Slavic Department&#8217;s information poster</a>.</p>
<p>Other institutions also offer programs in Russia and East European countries, both during the school year and summer. Please consult with the Major Advisor for information about these programs.</p>
<p>Read more about studying abroad on the <a href="http://eap.ucop.edu/Pages/default.aspx">Berkeley Programs for Study Abroad website.</a></p>
<p>Additionally, visit the website of UC Berkeley&#8217;s Institute for Slavic, East European, and Eurasian Studies (<a href="http://iseees.berkeley.edu/">ISEEES</a>), and their <a href="http://iseees.berkeley.edu/index.php?q=studyabroad">list of Study Abroad resources and options</a>.</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-1344 -->

										</div>

	</div><!-- #main -->

<article id="post-617" class="group post-container entry-content">
		<header class="index-header">
		<h1 class="index-title">
			TROIKA Undergrad Journal		</h1>
	
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->
	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p><a href="http://live-slavic.pantheon.berkeley.edu/wp-content/uploads/2014/06/Troika-Fall-2013-Front-Cover.jpg"><img class="wp-image-679 size-medium alignright" src="http://live-slavic.pantheon.berkeley.edu/wp-content/uploads/2014/06/Troika-Fall-2013-Front-Cover-252x300.jpg" alt="Troika Fall 2013 Front Cover" width="252" height="300" srcset="http://slavic.berkeley.edu/wp-content/uploads/2014/06/Troika-Fall-2013-Front-Cover-252x300.jpg 252w, http://slavic.berkeley.edu/wp-content/uploads/2014/06/Troika-Fall-2013-Front-Cover-768x913.jpg 768w, http://slavic.berkeley.edu/wp-content/uploads/2014/06/Troika-Fall-2013-Front-Cover-862x1024.jpg 862w, http://slavic.berkeley.edu/wp-content/uploads/2014/06/Troika-Fall-2013-Front-Cover-1200x1426.jpg 1200w" sizes="(max-width: 252px) 100vw, 252px" /></a>Dear Slavic Department and Students,</p>
<p>Want to be published?</p>
<p>Submit to the Troika Journal! UC Berkeley&#8217;s Undergraduate Journal in<br />
Slavic, Eastern European and Eurasian studies.<br />
We publish all kinds of work from undergraduates—academic writing, travel<br />
journals, fiction, poetry, art, and even photography.</p>
<p>For more information, please visit <a href="http://troika.berkeley.edu/submissions/">troika.berkeley.edu/submissions/</a><br />
or email us at <a href="mailto:thetroikajournal@gmail.com">thetroikajournal@gmail.com</a>.</p>
<p>Troika is sponsored by the Institute for Slavic, East European and<br />
Eurasian Studies as well as the Slavic Department and the ASUC.</p>
<p>Additional information and a pdf version of the first two issues of the<br />
journal are available on our website: <a href="http://troika.berkeley.edu/">http://troika.berkeley.edu/</a></p>
<p>There will also be print versions of the journal available in the Slavic<br />
Department. If you would like to request a copy of one, feel free to email<br />
us at thetroikajournal@gmail.com.</p>
<p>Thank you!</p>
<p>The Editors at Troika Journal</p>
<p>&#8212;&#8212;&#8212;&#8212;&#8212;&#8212;&#8212;&#8212;&#8212;&#8212;&#8212;&#8212;&#8212;&#8212;&#8212;&#8212;&#8212;&#8212;&#8212;&#8212;&#8212;&#8212;&#8212;&#8212;&#8212;-</p>
<p>Charlotte Pizzella<br />
Editor-in-Chief 2013-2014</p>
<p>Zuzanna Gruca and Isabella Mazzei<br />
Editors-in-Chief 2012-13</p>
<p>Olga Slobodyanyuk<br />
The Editor-in-Chief 2011-12</p>
<p>Alekzandir Morton<br />
The Founding Editor</p>
<p>Troika: An Undergraduate Journal in Slavic, East European and Eurasian<br />
Studies</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
